   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    T–V distinction
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=T-V_distinction&amp;redirect=no" title="T-V distinction">
       T-V distinction
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Sociolinguistics" title="Sociolinguistics">
        sociolinguistics
       </a>
       , a
       <b>
        T–V distinction
       </b>
       (from the Latin
       <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">
        pronouns
       </a>
       <i>
        <b>
         t
        </b>
        u
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <b>
         v
        </b>
        os
       </i>
       ) is a contrast, within one language, between various forms of addressing one's conversation partner or partners that are specialized for varying levels of politeness,
       <a href="/wiki/Social_distance" title="Social distance">
        social distance
       </a>
       , courtesy, familiarity, age or insult toward the addressee. Many languages lack this type of distinction, instead relying on more explicit wording to convey these meanings. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morphosyntax" title="Morphosyntax">
        morphosyntactic
       </a>
       T–V distinction, though, is found in a variety of languages around the world.
      </p>
      <p>
       Modern English technically has the T–V distinction, manifested in the pronouns
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       , though the familiar
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       is no longer used in most contemporary dialects. Additionally British commoners have historically spoken to nobility and royalty using the third person rather than the second person, a practice that has fallen out of favor. English speakers today often employ
       <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">
        semantic
       </a>
       analogues to convey the mentioned attitudes towards the addressee, such as whether to address someone by
       <a href="/wiki/Given_name" title="Given name">
        given
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">
        surname
       </a>
       , or whether to use
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sir" title="Sir">
         sir
        </a>
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Madam" title="Madam">
         ma'am
        </a>
       </i>
       . Under a broader classification,
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       forms are examples of
       <a href="/wiki/Honorifics_(linguistics)" title="Honorifics (linguistics)">
        honorifics
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The T–V distinction is expressed in a variety of forms. Two particularly common means are
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        Addressing a single individual using the second-person plural forms in the language, instead of the singular (e.g. in French).
       </li>
       <li>
        Addressing individuals in the third person, rather than the second (e.g. in Spanish).
       </li>
      </ol>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_usage">
        History and usage
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History and usage">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The terms
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       , based on the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       pronouns
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        vos
       </i>
       , were first used in a paper by the social psychologist
       <a href="/wiki/Roger_Brown_(psychologist)" title="Roger Brown (psychologist)">
        Roger Brown
       </a>
       and the Shakespearian scholar
       <a href="/wiki/Roger_Brown_(psychologist)#Personal_life" title="Roger Brown (psychologist)">
        Albert Gilman
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was a historical and contemporary survey of the uses of pronouns of address, seen as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semantic" title="Semantic">
        semantic
       </a>
       markers of social relationships between individuals. The study considered mainly
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       . The paper was highly influential
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and with few exceptions, the terms
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       have been used in subsequent studies.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">
        Origin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">
        Latin
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       was originally the singular, and
       <i>
        vos
       </i>
       the plural, with no distinction for honorific or familiar. According to Brown and Gilman, usage of the plural to the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">
        Roman emperor
       </a>
       began in the fourth century AD. They mention the possibility that this was because there were two emperors at that time (in
       <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">
        Constantinople
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">
        Rome
       </a>
       ), but also mention that "plurality is a very old and ubiquitous metaphor for power". This usage was extended to other powerful figures, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">
        Pope Gregory I
       </a>
       (590–604). However, Brown and Gilman note that it was only between the twelfth and fourteenth centuries that the norms for the use of T- and V-forms crystallized. Less commonly, the use of the plural may be extended to other
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
        persons
       </a>
       , such as the "
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_we" title="Royal we">
        royal we
       </a>
       " (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majestic_plural" title="Majestic plural">
        majestic plural
       </a>
       ) in
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Brown and Gilman argued that the choice of form is governed by either relationships of 'power' and/or 'solidarity', depending on the culture of the speakers, showing that 'power' had been the dominant predictor of form in Europe until the twentieth century. Thus, it was quite normal for a powerful person to use a T-form but expect a V-form in return. However, in the twentieth century the dynamic shifted in favour of solidarity, so that people would use T-forms with those they knew, and V-forms in service encounters, with
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/reciprocity" title="wikt:reciprocity">
        reciprocal
       </a>
       usage being the norm in both cases.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history:_the_power_semantic">
        Early history: the power semantic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early history: the power semantic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">
        Early Middle Ages
       </a>
       (the 5th century to the 10th century), the pronoun
       <i>
        vos
       </i>
       was used to address the most exalted figures, emperors and popes, who would use the pronoun
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       to address a subject. This use was progressively extended to other states and societies, and down the social hierarchy as a mark of respect to individuals of higher rank, religious authority, greater wealth, or seniority within a family. The development was slow and erratic, but a consistent pattern of use is estimated to have been reached in different European societies by the period 1100 to 1500. Use of V spread to upper-class individuals of equal rank, but not to lower class individuals.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This may be represented in Brown and Gilman's notation:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <table class="wikitable">
         <tr>
          <th colspan="2">
           Unequal power
          </th>
          <th colspan="2">
           Equal power
          </th>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           Emperor
          </td>
          <td>
           Father
          </td>
          <td>
           High-class friend
          </td>
          <td>
           Low-class friend
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           T↓
           <span class="nowrap">
           </span>
           ↑V
          </td>
          <td>
           T↓
           <span class="nowrap">
           </span>
           ↑V
          </td>
          <td>
           ↓↑V
          </td>
          <td>
           T↓↑
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           Subject
          </td>
          <td>
           Son
          </td>
          <td>
           High-class friend
          </td>
          <td>
           Low-class friend
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modification:_the_solidarity_semantic">
        Modification: the solidarity semantic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modification: the solidarity semantic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Speakers developed greater flexibility of pronoun use by redefining relationships between individuals. Instead of defining the father-son relationship as one of power, it could be seen as a shared family relationship. Brown and Gilman term this the semantics of solidarity. Thus a speaker might have a choice of pronoun, depending on how they perceived the relationship with the person addressed. Thus a speaker with superior power might choose
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       to express fellow feeling with a subordinate. For example, a restaurant customer might use
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       to their favourite waiter. Similarly a subordinate with a friendly relationship of long standing might use
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       . For example, a child might use
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       to express affection for his or her parent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This may be represented as:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <table class="wikitable">
         <tr>
          <th colspan="3">
           Superior has choice
          </th>
          <th colspan="3">
           Subordinate has choice
          </th>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           Customer
          </td>
          <td>
           Officer
          </td>
          <td>
           Employer
          </td>
          <td>
           Parent
          </td>
          <td>
           Master
          </td>
          <td>
           Elder sibling
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           T↓V
           <span class="nowrap">
           </span>
           ↑V
          </td>
          <td>
           T↓V
           <span class="nowrap">
           </span>
           ↑V
          </td>
          <td>
           T↓V
           <span class="nowrap">
           </span>
           ↑V
          </td>
          <td>
           T↓
           <span class="nowrap">
           </span>
           T↑V
          </td>
          <td>
           T↓
           <span class="nowrap">
           </span>
           T↑V
          </td>
          <td>
           T↓
           <span class="nowrap">
           </span>
           T↑V
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           Waiter
          </td>
          <td>
           Soldier
          </td>
          <td>
           Employee
          </td>
          <td>
           Child
          </td>
          <td>
           Faithful servant
          </td>
          <td>
           Younger sibling
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       These choices were available not only to reflect permanent relationships, but to express momentary changes of attitude. This allowed playwrights such as
       <a href="/wiki/Jean_Racine" title="Jean Racine">
        Racine
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Moli%C3%A8re" title="Molière">
        Molière
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">
        Ben Johnson
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">
        Marlowe
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare">
        Shakespeare
       </a>
       to express a character's inner changes of mood through outward changes of pronoun.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       For centuries, it was the more powerful individual who chose to address a subordinate either with
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       or with
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       , or to allow the subordinate to choose. For this reason, the pronouns were traditionally defined as the
       <i>
        pronoun of either condescension or intimacy
       </i>
       (
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       ) and
       <i>
        the pronoun of reverence or formality
       </i>
       (
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       ). Brown and Gilman argue that modern usage no longer supports these definitions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">
        Modern history
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Modern history">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Developments from the nineteenth century have seen the solidarity semantic more consistently applied. It has become less acceptable for a more powerful individual to exercise the choice of pronoun. Officers in most armies are not permitted to address a soldier as
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       . Most European parents cannot oblige their children to use
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       . The relationships illustrated above have changed in the direction of the following norms:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <table class="wikitable">
         <tr>
          <th colspan="3">
           Superior choice removed
          </th>
          <th colspan="3">
           Subordinate choice removed
          </th>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           Customer
          </td>
          <td>
           Officer
          </td>
          <td>
           Employer
          </td>
          <td>
           Parent
          </td>
          <td>
           Master
          </td>
          <td>
           Elder sibling
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           ↑↓V
          </td>
          <td>
           ↑↓V
          </td>
          <td>
           ↑↓V
          </td>
          <td>
           T↑↓
          </td>
          <td>
           T↑↓
          </td>
          <td>
           T↑↓
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td>
           Waiter
          </td>
          <td>
           Soldier
          </td>
          <td>
           Employee
          </td>
          <td>
           Child
          </td>
          <td>
           Faithful servant
          </td>
          <td>
           Younger sibling
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The tendency to promote the solidarity semantic may lead to the abolition of any choice of address pronoun. During the
       <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">
        French Revolution
       </a>
       attempts were made to abolish
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       . In seventeenth century England the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_of_Friends" title="Society of Friends">
        Society of Friends
       </a>
       obliged its members to use only
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       to everyone, and some continue to use
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       (
       <i>
        thee
       </i>
       ) to one another.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In most Modern English dialects the choice of
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       no longer exists outside of poetry.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_progress">
        Changes in progress
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Changes in progress">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       It was reported in 2012 that use of the
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       <i>
        usted
       </i>
       are in decline in
       <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">
        social media
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcnewsmag_lawn_2012_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbcnewsmag_lawn_2012-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An explanation offered was that such online communications favour the philosophy of
       <a href="/wiki/Social_equality" title="Social equality">
        equality
       </a>
       , regardless of usual formal distinctions. Similar tendencies were observed in
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">
        Persian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       as well as in
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcnewsmag_lawn_2012_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbcnewsmag_lawn_2012-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_use_in_individual_languages">
        History of use in individual languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History of use in individual languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="English">
        English
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: English">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
        Old English
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Middle_English#Early_Middle_English" title="Early Middle English">
        Early Middle English
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
        second person
       </a>
       pronouns
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ye
       </i>
       (with variants) were used for singular and plural reference respectively with no
       <b>
        T–V
       </b>
       distinction. The earliest entry in the
       <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">
        Oxford English Dictionary
       </a>
       for
       <i>
        ye
       </i>
       as a
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       pronoun in place of the singular
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       exists in a
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">
        Middle English
       </a>
       text of 1225 composed in 1200.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The usage may have started among the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_French" title="Norman French">
        Norman French
       </a>
       nobility in imitation of French. It made noticeable advances during the second half of the thirteenth century. During the sixteenth century, the distinction between the
       <a href="/wiki/Subject_(grammar)" title="Subject (grammar)">
        subject
       </a>
       form
       <i>
        ye
       </i>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Object_(grammar)" title="Object (grammar)">
        object
       </a>
       form
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       was largely lost, leaving
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       as the usual
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       pronoun (and plural pronoun). After 1600, the use of
       <i>
        ye
       </i>
       in standard English was confined to literary and religious contexts or as a consciously archaic usage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/David_Crystal" title="David Crystal">
        David Crystal
       </a>
       summarises
       <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">
        Early Modern English
       </a>
       usage thus:
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       would normally be used
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        by people of lower social status to those above them
       </li>
       <li>
        by the upper classes when talking to each other, even if they were closely related
       </li>
       <li>
        as a sign of a change (contrasting with
        <i>
         thou
        </i>
        ) in the emotional temperature of an interaction
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       would normally be used
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        by people of higher social status to those below them
       </li>
       <li>
        by the lower classes when talking to each other
       </li>
       <li>
        in addressing God
       </li>
       <li>
        in talking to ghosts, witches, and other supernatural beings
       </li>
       <li>
        in an imaginary address to someone who was absent
       </li>
       <li>
        as a sign of a change (contrasting with
        <i>
         you
        </i>
        ) in the emotional temperature of an interaction
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
         <a href="#cite_note-13">
          [13]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        T–V
       </b>
       distinction was still well preserved when Shakespeare began writing at the end of the sixteenth century. However, other playwrights of the time made less use of
       <b>
        T–V
       </b>
       contrasts than Shakespeare. The infrequent use of
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       in popular writing earlier in the century such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Paston_Letters" title="Paston Letters">
        Paston Letters
       </a>
       suggest that the distinction was already disappearing from gentry speech. In the first half of the seventeenth century,
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       disappeared from
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_English" title="Standard English">
        Standard English
       </a>
       , although the
       <b>
        T–V
       </b>
       distinction was preserved in many
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">
        regional dialects
       </a>
       . When the
       <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">
        Quakers
       </a>
       began using
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       again in the middle of the century, many people were still aware of the old
       <b>
        T–V
       </b>
       distinction and responded with derision and physical violence.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the nineteenth century, one aspect of the
       <b>
        T–V
       </b>
       distinction was restored to some English dialects in the form of a pronoun that expressed friendly solidarity, written as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Y%27all" title="Y'all">
         y'all
        </a>
       </i>
       . Unlike earlier
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       , it was used primarily for plural address, and in some dialects for singular address as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneider_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schneider-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The pronoun was first observed in the southern states of the US among
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American">
        African-American
       </a>
       speakers, although its precise origin is obscure. The pronoun spread rapidly to
       <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">
        White
       </a>
       speakers in those southern states, and (to a lesser extent) other regions of the US and beyond. This pronoun is not universally accepted, and may be regarded as either nonstandard or a regionalism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Yous(e) (pron. /juːz/, /jəz/) as a plural is found mainly in
       <a href="/wiki/English_language_in_Northern_England" title="English language in Northern England">
        (Northern) England
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_English" title="Scottish English">
        Scotland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-English" title="Hiberno-English">
        Ireland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">
        Australia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_English" title="New Zealand English">
        New Zealand
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">
        South Africa
       </a>
       , northern
       <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">
        Nova Scotia
       </a>
       and parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">
        Ontario
       </a>
       in Canada and parts of the northeastern United States (especially areas where there was historically Irish or Italian immigration), including in
       <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">
        Boston
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">
        Philadelphia
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York,_New_York" title="New York, New York">
        New York
       </a>
       , and scattered throughout working class Italian-American communities in the American
       <a href="/wiki/Rust_Belt" title="Rust Belt">
        Rust Belt
       </a>
       . It also occurs in
       <a href="/wiki/Scouse" title="Scouse">
        Scouse
       </a>
       (the regional dialect of the
       <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">
        Liverpool
       </a>
       area).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="French">
        French
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: French">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">
        Old French
       </a>
       texts, the pronouns
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       are often used interchangeably to address an individual, sometimes in the same sentence. However, some emerging pattern of use has been detected by recent scholars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Between characters equal in age or rank,
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       was more common than
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       as a singular address. However,
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       was sometimes used to put a young man in his place, or to express temporary anger. There may also have been variation between
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parisian_(person)" title="Parisian (person)">
        Parisian
       </a>
       use and that of other regions.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">
        Middle French
       </a>
       period a relatively stable
       <b>
        T–V
       </b>
       distinction emerged.
       <i>
        Vous
       </i>
       was the
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       form used by upper-class speakers to address one another, while
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       was the
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       form used among lower class speakers. Upper-class speakers could choose to use either
       <b>
        T
       </b>
       or
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       when addressing an inferior. Inferiors would normally use
       <b>
        V
       </b>
       to a superior. However, there was much variation; in 1596
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Pasquier" title="Étienne Pasquier">
        Étienne Pasquier
       </a>
       in his comprehensive survey
       <i>
        Recherches de la France
       </i>
       observed that the French sometimes used
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       to inferiors as well as to superiors "
       <i>
        selon la facilité de nos naturels
       </i>
       " ("according to our natural tendencies"). In poetry,
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       was often used to address kings or to speak to God.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_names">
        Use of names
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Use of names">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The boundaries between formal and informal language differ from language to language, as well as within social groups of the speakers of a given language. In some circumstances, it is not unusual to call other people by first name and the respectful form, or last name and familiar form. For example, German teachers use the former construct with upper-secondary students, while Italian teachers typically use the latter (switching to a full V-form with university students). This can lead to constructions denoting an intermediate level of formality in T–V-distinct languages that sound awkward to English-speakers. In Italian, "(Signor) Vincenzo Rossi" can be addressed with the tu (familiar) form or the Lei (formal) one, but complete addresses range from
       <i>
        Tu, Vincenzo
       </i>
       (peer to peer or family) and
       <i>
        Tu, Rossi
       </i>
       (teacher or fellow student to high-school student, as stated above) to
       <i>
        Lei, signor Vincenzo
       </i>
       (live-in servant to master or master's son) and
       <i>
        Lei, Rossi
       </i>
       (senior staff member to junior) and
       <i>
        Lei, signor Rossi
       </i>
       (among peers).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Translation_issues">
        Translation issues
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Translation issues">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The use of these forms may be an issue for
       <a href="/wiki/Untranslatability#Compensation" title="Untranslatability">
        compensating translation
       </a>
       of dialogue into English if the translator does not wish to use the "thou" pronoun to translate. For example, a character in a French film or novel saying "
       <i>
        Tutoie-moi!
       </i>
       " ("Use [the informal pronoun]
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       when addressing me!") might be translated "Do not be so formal!" or "Call me by my first name!"
      </p>
      <p>
       Conversely, when translating from English to a T–V language, the translator must decide again and again throughout the work which second-person form the reader would deem the more appropriate in a given situation. In the current German DVD release of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Gone_with_the_Wind_(film)" title="Gone with the Wind (film)">
         Gone with the Wind
        </a>
       </i>
       , the translators of the dubbed soundtrack and of the subtitles sometimes make opposite decisions; the actors dubbed voices speak with the familiar form, while the subtitles for the same scene are more formal.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Singular.2C_plural_and_other_ways_of_distinction">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Singular,_plural_and_other_ways_of_distinction">
        Singular, plural and other ways of distinction
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Singular, plural and other ways of distinction">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In many languages, the respectful singular pronoun derives from a plural form. Some Romance languages have familiar forms derived from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">
        Latin
       </a>
       singular
       <i>
        <b>
         t
        </b>
        u
       </i>
       and respectful forms derived from Latin plural
       <i>
        <b>
         v
        </b>
        os
       </i>
       , sometimes via a circuitous route. Sometimes, singular V-form derives from a third person pronoun; in
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       and some
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">
        Nordic
       </a>
       languages, it is the third person
       <i>
        plural
       </i>
       . Some languages have separate T and V forms for both singular and plural; others have the same form; others have a T–V distinction only in the singular.
      </p>
      <p>
       Different languages distinguish pronoun uses in different ways. Even within languages, there are differences between groups (older people and people of higher status tending both to use and to expect more respectful language) and between various aspects of one language. For example, in Dutch, V form
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       is slowly falling into disuse in the plural, thus one could sometimes address a group as T form
       <i>
        jullie
       </i>
       (which clearly expresses the plural) when one would address each member individually as
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       (which has the disadvantage of being ambiguous). In Latin American Spanish, the opposite change has occurred – having lost the T form
       <i>
        vosotros
       </i>
       , Latin Americans address all groups as
       <i>
        ustedes
       </i>
       , even if the group is composed of friends whom they would call
       <i>
        tú
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voseo" title="Voseo">
         vos
        </a>
       </i>
       (both T forms).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       In Standard Peninsular Spanish however,
       <i>
        vosotros
       </i>
       (literally, "you others") is still regularly employed in familiar conversation. In some cases V-forms are likely to be capitalized when written.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Table">
        Table
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">

         <i>
          kamu
         </i>
         (standard),
         <i>

         </i>
         (regional Malay; common spoken short form is
         <i>
          engkau
         </i>
         informal),
         <i>
          hang
         </i>
         (northern dialect, but understood and accepted across Peninsular Malaysia),
         <i>
          kau
         </i>
         (is impolite in all contexts except in very close relationships,
         <i>
          e.g.
         </i>
         friends [but not acquaintances])
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          anda
         </i>
         (polite/friendly formal; found in formal documents and in all formal contexts,
         <i>
          e.g.
         </i>
         advertisements.
         <i>
          Anda
         </i>
         is almost never encountered in spoken Malay; instead, most Malaysians would address a respected person by his title and/or name),
         <i>
          kamu
         </i>
         (unfriendly formal; also found in formal documents and in all formal contexts, where the intention is to convey a forceful tone in writing – often seen in
         <a href="/wiki/Lawsuit" title="Lawsuit">
          lawsuits
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Summons" title="Summons">
          summonses
         </a>
         ).
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          kamu semua
         </i>
         (polite/friendly formal),
         <i>
          kau orang
         </i>
         (when pronounced as
         <i>
          ko'rang
         </i>
         [used in very close relationships, equivalent to "you all" in parts of the U.S.] is slang and more informal),
         <i>
          hangpa
         </i>
         (northern dialect),
         <i>
          kalian

         </i>
         (to a man)
         <br/>
         <small>
          (verbs following any of the above addresses are in the 3rd person singular form)
          <br/>
          In the early period of the
          <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_(1945%E2%80%931989)" title="History of Poland (1945–1989)">
           communist rule
          </a>
          , a practice of using the second-person plural form
          <i>
           wy
          </i>
          (lit.
          <i>
           you
          </i>
          ) as a formal way of addressing a single person was introduced (a
          <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">
           calque
          </a>
          from Russian) but it did not catch on.
         </small>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          wy
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          państwo
         </i>
         (general)
         <br/>
         <i>
          panie
         </i>
         (to women)
         <br/>
         <i>
          panowie
         </i>
         (to men)
         <br/>
         <small>
          (verbs following any of the above addresses are in the 3rd person plural form, although in many cases for państwo (general) the 2nd person plural form is also possible).
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_specific_languages">
        In specific languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: In specific languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Germanic">
        Germanic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Germanic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Afrikaans">
        Afrikaans
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Afrikaans">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Modern
       <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">
        Afrikaans
       </a>
       rarely makes the distinction between the informal
       <i>
        jy
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        jou
       </i>
       ("you" subject and "your" / "you" object) and the more formal
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       (or
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       when addressing God), although sometimes it is upheld in a formal setting, such as in politics, business or in a polite conversation. The trend is moving towards using the informal pronoun most often.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dutch">
        Dutch
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Dutch">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Dutch" title="Old Dutch">
        Old Dutch
       </a>
       did not appear to have a T–V distinction.
       <i>
        Thu
       </i>
       was used as the second-person singular, and
       <i>
        gi
       </i>
       as the second-person plural. In early
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">
        Middle Dutch
       </a>
       , influenced by
       <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">
        Old French
       </a>
       usage, the original plural pronoun
       <i>
        gi
       </i>
       (or
       <i>
        ji
       </i>
       in the north) came to be used as a respectful singular pronoun, creating a T–V distinction. However, the formal
       <i>
        gi
       </i>
       started to be used in more and more situations. By the 17th century,
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       had largely fallen out of use, although it lingered on in some of the more peripheral areas. At this point, the original T–V distinction had been lost, and the original V-pronoun
       <i>
        gij
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        jij
       </i>
       was used universally for both singular and plural regardless of the type of address. This resembled the state of English today, which has also (outside of dialectal, literary or religious use) lost its original T-pronoun
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Around this time, a new formal pronoun
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       started to come into use. This was also the object form of the subject pronoun
       <i>
        gij
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        jij
       </i>
       , and how it came to be used as a subject pronoun is not exactly clear. It is usually related to a form of address in writing of the time: letters were often addressed formally to
       <i>
        U.E.
       </i>
       , standing for
       <i>
        Uwe Edelheid
       </i>
       ("Your Highness"), which is thought to have been shortened to
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       eventually. It can be compared to the Spanish
       <i>
        usted
       </i>
       , which is a similar contraction of a phrase of indirect address. As in Spanish, the Dutch
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       was originally conjugated as the third person in verbs, although most verbs had identical second- and third-person singular forms, so that this difference was not apparent for the most part. It remains today in the use of
       <i>
        u heeft
       </i>
       ("you (formal) have", like
       <i>
        hij heeft
       </i>
       "he has"), compared to
       <i>
        jij hebt
       </i>
       ("you (informal) have"). However,
       <i>
        u hebt
       </i>
       is now also common.
      </p>
      <p>
       Around the same time, it became more common to clarify when multiple people were being spoken to, by adding
       <i>
        luyden
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        lieden
       </i>
       ("people"), or a shortened variety, to the end of the pronoun. Thus, when speaking to multiple people, one would use
       <i>
        jij luyden
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        je lieden
       </i>
       . This combination was contracted and fused over time, eventually resulting in
       <i>
        jullie
       </i>
       , the informal plural pronoun that is used today. It can be compared, in its origin, to the English
       <i>
        y'all
       </i>
       or Spanish
       <i>
        vosotros
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Modern northern
       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">
        Dutch
       </a>
       , and usually standard Dutch as well, has two forms of second person pronouns, namely
       <i>
        jij
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       .
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       is the formal pronoun, whereas
       <i>
        jij
       </i>
       is used as the informal personal pronoun to address a single person. In the plural,
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       is also used, alongside the informal
       <i>
        jullie
       </i>
       . In the south, only one pronoun,
       <i>
        gij
       </i>
       , is generally used in all three roles: both singular and plural, formal and informal.
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       is sometimes also used in formal situations, but the southern
       <i>
        gij
       </i>
       does not have a distinct informal connotation like the northern/standard
       <i>
        jij
       </i>
       , and can be used to address anyone without offence. Religious Dutch speakers in all areas address
       <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
        God
       </a>
       using either
       <i>
        Gij
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        jij
       </i>
       is never used. For speakers of the north, this is usually the only place where
       <i>
        gij
       </i>
       is encountered, giving it a formal and archaic tone, even though it is neutral in the southern areas where it is still used.
      </p>
      <p>
       The pronoun
       <i>
        je
       </i>
       (unstressed variant of
       <i>
        jij
       </i>
       ) can also be used impersonally, corresponding to the English
       <a href="/wiki/Generic_you" title="Generic you">
        generic you
       </a>
       . The more formal Dutch term corresponding to English
       <a href="/wiki/Generic_you" title="Generic you">
        generic you
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generic_one" title="Generic one">
        one
       </a>
       is
       <i>
        men
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In Dutch the formal personal pronoun is used for older people or for people with a higher or equal status, unless the addressed makes it clear they want to be spoken to with the informal pronoun. Unlike for example in
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       , there is no defined line (in the case of German, roughly when someone passes the age of 16) in which everyone, apart from family, is addressed with the formal pronoun. Addressing parents by
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       has become very rare;
       <i>
        jij
       </i>
       is often even used to address grandparents. There is also a tendency towards more use of the informal pronoun. Some companies such as
       <a href="/wiki/IKEA" title="IKEA">
        IKEA
       </a>
       consciously address their customers with the informal
       <i>
        jij
       </i>
       . However,
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       can still be considered more or less obligatory in situations where, for example, a pupil addresses his teacher, people testify in court or communication between a doctor and his patient.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="English_2">
        English
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: English">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Thou" title="Thou">
        Thou
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/You" title="You">
        You
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Contemporary English generally uses only the form "you", regardless of level of familiarity.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
        Old English
       </a>
       used
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C3%BE%C5%AB" title="wikt:þū">
         þū
        </a>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in the second-person singular for both formal and informal contexts. Following the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" title="Norman Conquest">
        Norman Conquest
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">
        Middle English
       </a>
       that emerged continued to use
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C3%BEou" title="wikt:þou">
         þou
        </a>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       at first, but by the 13th century, Norman
       <a href="#French">
        French
       </a>
       influence had led to the use of the second-person plural
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C8%9De" title="wikt:ȝe">
         ȝe
        </a>
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ye" title="wikt:ye">
         ye
        </a>
       </i>
       in formal contexts.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">
        Early Modern English
       </a>
       superiors and strangers were therefore respectfully addressed as
       <i>
        ye
       </i>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">
        nominative
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yeyou_25-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-yeyou-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Objective_case" title="Objective case">
        objective
       </a>
       ;
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        thee
       </i>
       were used for familiars and subordinates. The more widespread and observed this division became, the more pejorative it became to strangers to be called by the familiar form of address. By the 17th century, such a use among the nobility was strongly and deliberately contemptuous, as in the declamation of the prosecutor at Sir
       <a href="/wiki/Walter_Raleigh" title="Walter Raleigh">
        Walter Raleigh
       </a>
       's 1603 trial "I thou thee, thou traitor!" Accordingly, the use of
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       began to decline and it was effectively extinct in the everyday speech of most English dialects by the early 18th century, supplanted by the polite
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       , even when addressing children and animals, something also seen in Dutch and Latin America (most of
       <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese#Tu_and_você" title="Brazilian Portuguese">
        Brazil
       </a>
       and parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language#Usted" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish America
       </a>
       ). Meanwhile, as part of English's continuing development away from its
       <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_language" title="Synthetic language">
        synthetic
       </a>
       origins since the influx of French vocabulary following the Norman invasion,
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       had been replacing
       <i>
        ye
       </i>
       since the 15th century. Standard English was left with a single second-person pronoun for all cases, numbers and contexts and largely incapable of maintaining a T–V distinction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Notwithstanding all of this, the translators of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_James_version" title="King James version">
        King James version
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">
        Bible
       </a>
       chose to employ the older forms in their work (1604–1611) in order to convey the grammatical distinctions made by their
       <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Bomberg" title="Daniel Bomberg">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Beza" title="Theodore Beza">
        Greek
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Estienne" title="Robert Estienne">
        Latin
       </a>
       sources. Its subsequent popularity and the religious rationale of many
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       who continued to employ
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       has preserved its use in English, but made it seem pious and (ironically)
       <i>
        more
       </i>
       formal and respectful than the everyday
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the United States, some Protestant sects, such as the Quakers (Society of Friends) and Mormons would insist on addressing everyone as
       <i>
        thou
       </i>
       , because they considered every person to be a friend and an equal. This persisted until the 19th or early 20th century.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Frisian_.28West.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Frisian_(West)">
        Frisian (West)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Frisian (West)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/West_Frisian_language" title="West Frisian language">
        West Frisian
       </a>
       , the formal singular nominative
       <i>
        jo
       </i>
       (pronounced
       <i>
        yo
       </i>
       ) is very close to the English
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       and the Middle and Early Modern English
       <i>
        ye
       </i>
       . There is no such distinction in the plural; the plural second person pronoun is always
       <i>
        jimme
       </i>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stadsfries" title="Stadsfries">
        Stadsfries
       </a>
       , a Dutch dialect with strong Frisian influence, parallels this distinction (
       <i>
        dou, jou, jimme
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       West Frisian
       <i>
        jo
       </i>
       is used slightly more often than Dutch
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       . Native speakers of Dutch are sometimes warned against addressing newly acquainted people with
       <i>
        do
       </i>
       to soon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="German">
        German
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: German">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sie_and_du">
        <i>
         Sie
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         du
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sie and du">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Du_(personal_pronoun)" title="Du (personal pronoun)">
        Du (personal pronoun)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       , the formal address
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       is the same as the third person plural pronoun
       <i>
        sie
       </i>
       . Verbs used with this form of address are also identical to third person plural forms. The polite form and its inflected forms are always capitalized in writing, to avoid any ambiguity.
      </p>
      <p>
       The corresponding informal German address is
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       . The verbs
       <i>
        duzen
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        siezen
       </i>
       mean respectively "to address using du" and "to address using Sie" and the phrases
       <i>
        per du
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        auf du und du
       </i>
       mean, "to be on du terms". The use of
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       often coincides with the use of the title plus surname,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siedu_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Siedu-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       usage of which is more widespread in German-speaking areas than Anglophone areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siedu_37-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Siedu-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In general terms,
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       is used to children, animals and God, and between adults (or between adults and children) who are good friends of or closely related to each other.
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       is used in other situations, such as in a business situation or where there is no existing relationship.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siedu_37-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Siedu-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Internet chats and forums, Germans rarely use
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       , although there are exceptions. Except in the case of adults addressing children, where it is common for the child to address the adult as
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       , but be addressed as
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       in return, it is not common in German for one party to address the other as
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       , but be addressed as
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       in return.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siedu_37-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Siedu-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In almost all cases it can be considered as impolite to use the "wrong" pronoun, i. e. a pronoun that is not expected by the other party.
      </p>
      <p>
       High school students in Germany are often called
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       plus given name ("Hamburger Sie") by their teachers when they enter the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Abitur" title="Abitur">
         Oberstufe
        </a>
       </i>
       – the last 2 or 3 years of high school – around the age of 16.
      </p>
      <p>
       Children and teenagers are expected to use
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       when addressing all adults except for family members and family friends whom they have known since early childhood. Street and similar social workers, sports clubs trainers will sometimes tell children and teens to address them with
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       . In shops, bars, and other establishments, if they target a younger audience, it is becoming increasingly common for customers and staff to address each other as
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       , to the degree that it is sometimes considered awkward if a waitress and a customer who are both in their twenties call each other
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The use of
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       between two strangers may also be determined by the setting in which they meet (casual/formal), as well as clothing (casual/formal), gender (same/opposite), and personal preference. For example, it is customary to use
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       in traditional small pubs and taverns in certain regions (including the
       <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">
        Rhineland
       </a>
       ). This applies also to older people, whom one would otherwise address as
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       . Two people who addressed each other as
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       in a pub may go back to
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       when they meet in the street if their acquaintance was only very superficial. During the famous Rhenish
       <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">
        carnival
       </a>
       , it is customary for most revelers to address each other as
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       . Only if the age difference is more than one generation, the younger person might still use
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       . Another setting in which
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       is often used between adults is sporting events. As a learned social trait, one person would normally use
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       , while he or she asks the other person if it is acceptable to be addressed informally, and then act accordingly.
      </p>
      <p>
       Being
       <i>
        per du
       </i>
       has also become increasingly common in workplace environments (depending on the line of business and corporate culture to varying degrees), mostly regardless of age. In such environments, the
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       basis may also be observed as a (sometimes necessary) mark of good social integration within a working group. As a rule of thumb, one might expect to see team colleagues on the workplace level in many industries on a customary
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       basis with each other, though not always with the group manager and more rarely with higher-ranking managers. As entrants to a team are more closely integrated, this is often marked by making an informal affirmation to that basis or by formally offering it, as a matter of style and habituality. Both the tempo and extent of using the
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       basis depends much on the culture (and sometimes the climate) of the business, and in some places even more so on that of the particular workgroup itself. Business cultures that pride themselves on a "flat hierarchy" are more likely to adopt or accent a general professional parlance of
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       and given name while inside corporations tending to emphasize professional formality, a
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       may be expected to be used always except between very close colleagues or inside closed groups (sometimes including managers meeting on the same level with the exclusion of any subordinates), and strictly always in the presence of a superior. The superior, on the other hand, has the right to address the other perform informally or formally, which is a personal preference.
      </p>
      <p>
       Customarily, the switch from
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       is initially proposed by the elder of the two people, the person with socially higher standing or by the lady to the gentleman. One way to propose the use of
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       rather than
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       is by stating one's first name (as in:
       <i>
        Ich heiße...
       </i>
       ). One accepts the proposal by introducing one's own first name. Should a person later forget that they have adopted
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       , it is polite to remind them by saying,
       <i>
        Wir waren doch per du
       </i>
       (We moved on to 'du' terms). Sometimes switching back to
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       is used as a method of distancing oneself from the addressee; the connotation is slightly ironic courtesy.
      </p>
      <p>
       The inappropriate and uninvited use of
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       towards someone who would otherwise reasonably expect to be addressed as
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       is considered to be condescending and disrespectful, although insistence on
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       in an environment where
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       is largely accepted (flat hierarchies) can be interpreted as being equally disrespectful. The degree of offense that might be taken will depend on how obvious the etiquette violation was (an example of an obvious violation would be a teenager in the street addressing an elderly stranger on the street with
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       , addressing a senior manager with
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       as a result of a misjudged professional relationship would probably be taken with less offense), and will also depend on the upbringing of the person in question – progressive vs. conservative outlooks and age are examples of factors which can play a role in how individuals prefer to be addressed and choose to address others.
      </p>
      <p>
       It has become the policy of some businesses for their employees to address customers with
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       , often to set a progressive, "modern" tone, occasionally for other cultural reasons.
       <a href="/wiki/IKEA" title="IKEA">
        IKEA
       </a>
       , for instance, does this to reflect the widespread use of the
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       form in Sweden (see
       <a href="/wiki/Du-reformen" title="Du-reformen">
        Du-reformen
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       In Germany, an old
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norm_(sociology)" title="Norm (sociology)">
        custom
       </a>
       (called
       <i>
        Bruderschaft trinken
       </i>
       , drinking brotherhood) involves two friends formally sharing a bottle of
       <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">
        wine
       </a>
       or drinking a glass of
       <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">
        beer
       </a>
       together to celebrate their agreement to call one another
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       rather than
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       . This custom has also been adopted among the Swiss-French of the Jura, in Poland and Russia (called by its German name, spelled
       <i>
        bruderszaft
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        брудершафт
       </i>
       respectively), though the custom in Poland is now slowly disappearing. It was formerly found also in Sweden.
      </p>
      <p>
       Although the use of
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       generally coincides with the use of title plus surname, especially in northern and eastern Germany, there is an intermediate address combining
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       with the first name ("
       <a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamburger_Sie" title="de:Hamburger Sie">
        Hamburger Sie
       </a>
       "), whereas in the Berlin region, sometimes
       <i>
        Du
       </i>
       is combined with the surname ("Berliner Du"). The former usage also occurs when addressing teenagers, household staff, or guests of TV or radio programs, while the latter style is usually considered inferior and mainly occurs in working class environments. It may be associated with professional contexts, when colleagues have known one another for a long time, but, e. g. due to differences of status, do not want to switch to the usual
       <i>
        Du
       </i>
       style; or in situations where strangers (e.g. customers) are present for whom it would not be appropriate to learn the first name of the addressee.
      </p>
      <p>
       When speaking to more than one person in formal situations,
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       is used in standard German, although
       <i>
        ihr
       </i>
       can often be heard instead, especially in the South of Germany and in
       <a href="/wiki/Swiss_German" title="Swiss German">
        Swiss German dialects
       </a>
       . Usage varies when addressing a group containing both
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       persons from the speaker's point of view. Some speakers use the informal plural
       <i>
        ihr
       </i>
       , others prefer the formal
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       and many, concerned that both pronouns might cause offence, prefer to use circumlocutions that avoid either pronoun, for example by expressing an
       <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">
        imperative
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">
        infinitive
       </a>
       form (
       <i>
        bitte das machen
       </i>
       ), by applying the
       <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">
        passive voice
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        es wird gemacht
       </i>
       ), or using the
       <a href="/wiki/Generic_you" title="Generic you">
        indefinite pronoun
       </a>
       <i>
        man
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        man macht das
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span id="Historical_predecessors:_Ihr_and_Er.2FSie">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_predecessors:_Ihr_and_Er/Sie">
        Historical predecessors:
        <i>
         Ihr
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         Er/Sie
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Historical predecessors: Ihr and Er/Sie">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Formerly, the 2nd person plural
       <i>
        Ihr
       </i>
       ("ye") was used to address social superiors, unless more informal relations had been established. The use of
       <i>
        Ihr
       </i>
       as the polite form, as this is the case with
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       in the French language, has still survived in
       <a href="/wiki/Bernese_German" title="Bernese German">
        Bernese German
       </a>
       and other
       <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">
        alemannic
       </a>
       dialects.
       <i>
        Ihr
       </i>
       in this case has to be capitalized. However, Ihr itself shows a degree of informality, and would for example be used in addressing one's father. For the formal address, the third person would be used; and this in the singular with Er, Sie (capitalized) to a social inferior, as a farmer addressing a stableboy, or in the plural to a social superior. It is from the latter occurrences that modern
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       takes its origin;
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       is the 3rd person plural pronoun. However,
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       itself is relatively young, and it was rather the formal addresses, often itself singular forms, that took the plural. Even as late as in
       <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_D%C3%BCrrenmatt" title="Friedrich Dürrenmatt">
        Dürrenmatt's
       </a>
       "The Visit" (written in 1956), an address
       <i>
        Das wissen Herr Bürgermeister schon
       </i>
       ("You do know that, Mr. Mayor", modern German would just say
       <i>
        Das wissen Sie schon
       </i>
       ) can be found; Herr Bürgermeister is the formal address and itself a singular term, but wissen is plural. However, if the formal address itself contains a personal pronoun as in
       <i>
        Seine Majestät
       </i>
       (His Majesty) etc., this one would be put to the 2nd person plural:
       <i>
        Was geruhen Euer
       </i>
       (not:
       <i>
        Seine
       </i>
       )
       <i>
        Majestät zu befehlen?
       </i>
       ("What does [but plural] Your Majesty condescend to order?")
      </p>
      <p>
       Thus, all these go by a similar grammar rule pertaining to the verb used with these addresses as modern
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       . The dated capitalized address
       <i>
        Ihr
       </i>
