       demands the same verb form as the modern second person plural pronoun
       <i>
        ihr
       </i>
       , the dated
       <i>
        Er/Sie
       </i>
       demands the same verb form as the modern third person singular
       <i>
        er
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        sie
       </i>
       , and the dated 3rd person plural address without
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       demands, just as
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       itself, the same verb form as the 3rd person plural pronoun
       <i>
        sie
       </i>
       (they).
      </p>
      <p>
       The forms are still found today in some dialects as a respectful way of addressing elders, and is still very often found in works of art and literature (such as books and movies) depicting events at least several centuries in the past, or in a "past-like" fantasy setting, even if modern German is otherwise used in these works; indeed, using the modern
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       in such a setting would be considered an out-of-place anachronism.
       <i>
        Ihr
       </i>
       and the 3rd person plural without
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       is somewhat analogous to the English
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majestic_plural" title="Majestic plural">
        majestic plural
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Er, Sie
       </i>
       form is not widely known or understood by the average person any more. While
       <i>
        Ihrzen
       </i>
       is often still used in dubbed films, especially in medieval/fantasy contexts such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Rings" title="Lord of the Rings">
        Lord of the Rings
       </a>
       e.g. "
       <i>
        Ihr
       </i>
       habt das Reich der Herrin des Waldes betreten,
       <i>
        Ihr
       </i>
       könnt nicht umkehren" ("you have entered the Realm of the Lady of the Wood, you can not turn back"). In this context, a historical level is used where the second person plural indicates some nobility of or respect for the addressee, such that from
       <i>
        Ihr
       </i>
       being used to address a single person, the viewer could mostly, without looking, conclude that the person was of elevated rank such as a king or nobleman, or at least being treated with expressed regard.
       <i>
        Ihr
       </i>
       would not normally be used to address a peasant (unless he is a prince in disguise or a future prince and the person addressing him has gathered some knowledge or presumption of that).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Scandinavian_languages">
        Scandinavian languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Scandinavian languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Danish">
        Danish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Danish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">
        Danish
       </a>
       , the informal second-person singular is
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/du" title="wikt:du">
         du
        </a>
       </i>
       and the formal form of address uses the third-person plural
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/De" title="wikt:De">
         De
        </a>
       </i>
       , capitalized to distinguish it from its other use. The second-person plural
       <i>
        I
       </i>
       and the third-person singular
       <i>
        han
       </i>
       ("he") or
       <i>
        hun
       </i>
       ("she") were sometimes used until the early 19th century in standard Danish
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and awhile longer in the countryside. The German-inspired form
       <i>
        De
       </i>
       entered Danish in the 18th century, too late to enter liturgical use. In church, as in rural or dialect-speaking areas,
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       has always been the universal form, especially in
       <a href="/wiki/Jutland" title="Jutland">
        Jutland
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       As in other Scandinavian languages, even among the prestige dialects, the formal pronoun is waning in use – in the case of Danish, since the
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://da.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ungdomsopr%C3%B8ret_i_Danmark" title="da:Ungdomsoprøret i Danmark">
         Ungdomsoprøret
        </a>
       </i>
       ("Youth Revolts") during and after the
       <a href="/wiki/Protests_of_1968" title="Protests of 1968">
        protests of 1968
       </a>
       . As a general rule, the informal
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       is accepted everywhere today, except when addressing
       <a href="/wiki/Danish_royal_family" title="Danish royal family">
        royalty
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or during military service. In other contexts, it has come to seem excessively formal and old-fashioned to most Danes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Even at job interviews and among
       <a href="/wiki/Folketing" title="Folketing">
        parliamentarians
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       has become standard.
      </p>
      <p>
       In written Danish,
       <i>
        De
       </i>
       remains current in legal, legislative, and formal business documents, as well as in some translations from other languages. This is sometimes audience-dependent, as in the Danish government's general use of
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       except in healthcare information directed towards the elderly,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       where
       <i>
        De
       </i>
       is still used. Other times, it is maintained as an affectation, as by the staff of some formal restaurants, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Weekendavisen" title="Weekendavisen">
         Weekendavisen
        </a>
       </i>
       newspaper,
       <a href="/wiki/TV_2_(Denmark)" title="TV 2 (Denmark)">
        TV 2
       </a>
       announcers, and the avowedly conservative
       <a href="/wiki/Maersk" title="Maersk">
        Maersk
       </a>
       corporation. Attempts by other corporations to avoid sounding either stuffy or too informal by employing circumlocutions – using
       <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">
        passive phrasing
       </a>
       or using the pronoun
       <i>
        man
       </i>
       ("one") – have generally proved awkward and been ill-received,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and (with the notable exception of the national railway
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danske_Statsbaner" title="Danske Statsbaner">
        DSB
       </a>
       ) most have opted for the more personable
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       form.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Icelandic">
        Icelandic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Icelandic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Modern
       <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">
        Icelandic
       </a>
       is the Scandinavian dialect closest to
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">
        Old Norse
       </a>
       , which made a distinction between the plural
       <i>
        þér
       </i>
       and the dual
       <i>
        þið
       </i>
       . This distinction continued in written Icelandic the early 1920 when the plural
       <i>
        þér
       </i>
       was also used on formal occasions. The formal usage of
       <i>
        þér
       </i>
       seems to have pushed the dual
       <i>
        þið
       </i>
       to take over the plural so modern Icelandic normally uses
       <i>
        þið
       </i>
       as a plural. However, in formal documents such as by the president
       <i>
        þér
       </i>
       is still used as plural, and the usage of þér as plural and þið as dual is still retained in the Icelandic translation of the Christian scriptures. There are still a number of fixed expressions – particularly religious adages such as "seek and ye shall find" (
       <i>
        leitið og þér munuð finna
       </i>
       ) – and the formal pronoun is sometimes used in translations from a language that adheres to a T–V distinction, but otherwise it appears only when one wants to be excessively formal either from the gravity of the occasion (as in court proceedings and legal correspondence) or out of contempt (in order to ridicule another person's self-importance), and
       <i>
        þú
       </i>
       is used in all other cases.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Norwegian">
        Norwegian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Norwegian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">
        Norwegian
       </a>
       , the polite form
       <i>
        De
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        Dem
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Bokm%C3%A5l" title="Bokmål">
        Bokmål
       </a>
       ) and
       <i>
        De
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        Dykk
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Nynorsk" title="Nynorsk">
        Nynorsk
       </a>
       ) has more or less disappeared in both spoken and written language. Norwegians now exclusively use
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       , and the polite form does not have a strong cultural pedigree in the country. Until recently,
       <i>
        De
       </i>
       would sometimes be found in written works, business letters, plays and translations where an impression of formality must be retained. The popular belief that
       <i>
        De
       </i>
       is reserved for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Norway" title="King of Norway">
        king
       </a>
       is incorrect, since according to royal etiquette, the King (and other members of the royal family) will be addressed as
       <i>
        Deres majestet
       </i>
       (bokmål)/
       <i>
        Dykkar majestet
       </i>
       (nynorsk) (Your majesty) or in third person singular as
       <i>
        Hans majestet
       </i>
       (His majesty),
       <i>
        Hennes majestet
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        Hennar majestet
       </i>
       (Her majesty),
       <i>
        Kongen
       </i>
       (the King),
       <i>
        Dronningen
       </i>
       (the Queen) and similar.
      </p>
      <p>
       Norwegians generally refer to one another by first name only, unless the person is better known by full or last name only. This also contributes to the weakening of these pronouns and a general pattern of declining use of polite speech. For example, a student might address his professor by his first name, but would refer to a leading politician by his last name. Norwegian politicians and celebrities are sometimes referred to by their first names, especially in newspaper headlines, while the text of the article most likely would use the person's last name. Nicknames are not very common.
      </p>
      <p>
       The distinction between
       <a href="/wiki/Bokm%C3%A5l" title="Bokmål">
        Bokmål
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Nynorsk" title="Nynorsk">
        Nynorsk
       </a>
       exists primarily for written Norwegian (most Norwegians speak dialects that differ from the standard written forms), and the T–V rules are the same for both forms—except that Bokmål uses the third person plural to indicate politeness (as in German), while Nynorsk uses the second person plural (as in French). In both forms, when these pronouns are used to indicate politeness, they are always capitalised (to show deference, and separate them from when they indicate, respectively, the third and second person plural).
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Swedish">
        Swedish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Swedish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Du-reformen" title="Du-reformen">
        Du-reformen
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">
        Swedish
       </a>
       , there has in the last two centuries been a marked difference between usage in
       <a href="/wiki/Finland_Swedish" title="Finland Swedish">
        Finland Swedish
       </a>
       and in
       <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">
        Sweden
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the Swedish of Sweden, the polite
       <i>
        Ni
       </i>
       was known from earlier epochs, but had come to be considered somewhat careless, bullying or rude; instead, an intricate system had evolved in order to prudently step around pronouns almost altogether. Parts of this system began to erode around the Second World War or so, but the essentials held up into the 1960s.
      </p>
      <p>
       As the twentieth century progressed, this circumlocutive system of addressing, with its innumerable ambiguities and opportunities for unintentional offence, was increasingly felt as a nuisance. In the sixties, the so-called
       <a href="/wiki/Du-reformen" title="Du-reformen">
        du-reformen
       </a>
       ('thou-reform') was carried out. First, authorities and influential circles tried rehabilitating the
       <i>
        Ni
       </i>
       in a so-called "
       <i>
        ni
       </i>
       reform"—but most people could not bring themselves to feel civil using that. Then, almost overnight and dubbed the "
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       reform", the system broke down, and
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       (noted as informal above) became the accepted way of addressing any one person except royalty.
      </p>
      <p>
       Addressing royalty went somewhat more slowly from a universal
       <i>
        Ers majestät
       </i>
       ('Your Majesty'), etc., to that address only on formal occasions, otherwise replaced by third person (singular if the addressee is single) with title (
       <i>
        K(on)ungen
       </i>
       'the King', etc.).
      </p>
      <p>
       These rules still apply, with marginal exceptions. The vast majority of Swedes, including younger people in most or all situations, stick to the
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       . In order to "alleviate the intrusion" in writing, e.g. in letters or in advertisement, the
       <i>
        Du
       </i>
       can be capitalized. That usage was most widespread in the early days of universal
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       address; it has become slightly more common again simultaneously with the partial
       <i>
        Ni
       </i>
       revival.
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland Swedish has undergone a similar development to mainland Swedish since the 1960s, but slower and slightly less. There, one may have to reckon with influence from the
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish language
       </a>
       , still slightly more conservative. In Finland Swedish, the second person plural form
       <i>
        Ni
       </i>
       (noted as formal above) was indeed the traditional respectful address to a single person up to the 1970s or so.
      </p>
      <p>
       Swedish, also, has verbs for the addresses:
       <i>
        dua
       </i>
       'to say
       <i>
        du
       </i>
       ', and
       <i>
        nia
       </i>
       'to say
       <i>
        ni
       </i>
       '.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Scots">
        Scots
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Scots">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Scots" title="Modern Scots">
        Modern Scots
       </a>
       the second person singular nominative
       <i>
        thoo
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ðuː]
       </span>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Scots" title="Southern Scots">
        Southern Scots
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ðʌu]
       </span>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shetlandic" title="Shetlandic">
        Shetlandic
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [duː]
       </span>
       ) survived in colloquial speech until the mid 19th century in most of lowland Scotland.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       It has since been replaced by
       <i>
        ye
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       in most areas except in
       <a href="/wiki/Insular_Scots" title="Insular Scots">
        Insular Scots
       </a>
       where
       <i>
        thee
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ðiː]
       </span>
       , Shetlandic
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [diː]
       </span>
       ) is also used, in
       <a href="/wiki/North_Northern_Scots" title="North Northern Scots">
        North Northern Scots
       </a>
       and in some Southern Scots varieties.
       <i>
        Thoo
       </i>
       is used as the familiar form by parents speaking to children, elders to youngsters, or between friends or equals. The second person formal singular
       <i>
        ye
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       is used when speaking to a superior or when a youngster addresses an elder. The older second person singular possessive
       <i>
        thy
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ðai]
       </span>
       ), and
       <i>
        thee
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ði]
       </span>
       , Shetlandic
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [diː]
       </span>
       along with
       <i>
        thine
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       )
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dəin(z)]
       </span>
       ) still survive to some extent where
       <i>
        thoo
       </i>
       remains in use.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Yiddish">
        Yiddish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Yiddish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yiddish_language" title="Yiddish language">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       makes use of the second person plural form as the polite form for both singular and plural. In the second person plural form
       <span lang="yi">
        איר
       </span>
       <i>
        (ir)
       </i>
       , there is therefore no distinction between formal and informal forms. There is a
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">
        dialectal
       </a>
       pronoun
       <span lang="yi">
        עץ
       </span>
       <i>
        (ets)
       </i>
       strictly for informal second-person plural form, but this pronoun is rarely used today and is only found in some dialects of Poland and neighboring regions.
      </p>
      <p>
       Given that old German dialects were the main influence on the development of the Yiddish language, this form may be recognized with older polite forms of the German language.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2008)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Romance_languages">
        Romance languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Romance languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="French_2">
        French
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: French">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In most
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       -speaking regions (Canada is an exception; see "
       <a href="#North_American_French">
        North American French
       </a>
       " below), a rigid T–V distinction is upheld. With regard to the second person singular,
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is used informally, whereas
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       is used to convey formality. (The second person plural is always
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       .) The formal
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       is expected when encountering any unknown adult under normal circumstances. In general, the switch from
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is "negotiated" on a case-by-case basis; it can happen nearly unconsciously, or can be explicitly negotiated. For instance, some couples have been known to call each other
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       for some time while dating, and gradually switch to calling each other
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       . The verb
       <i>
        tutoyer
       </i>
       means "address someone with
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       -forms, speak informally"; by contrast
       <i>
        vouvoyer
       </i>
       means "address someone with
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       forms". Rigidly sticking to
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       can become equally awkward in a long-standing relationship.
      </p>
      <p>
       In certain circumstances, however,
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is used more broadly. For example, new acquaintances who are conscious of having something socially significant in common (e.g., student status, or the same "rank" in some hierarchy) often use
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       more or less immediately. In some cases, there may be an explicitly defined practice in a particular company, political party, as to the use of
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       . Also, using the
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       in conjunction with someone's given name is rather current in France as a less formal way of addressing someone, e.g. at work, among members of an association etc. Children and adolescents generally use
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       to speak with someone of their own age, whether known or not.
       <i>
        Tu
       </i>
       can also be used to show disrespect to a stranger, such as when surprising a thief or cursing other drivers on the road.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Vous
       </i>
       may be used to distance oneself from a person with whom one does not want to interact. Additionally, two people who use
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       in their private interactions may consciously switch back to
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       in public in order to act appropriately in a formal or professional environment, to play the part in an artificially constructed situation (e.g., co-hosts of a television show), or simply to conceal the nature of their relationship from others.
      </p>
      <p>
       In families,
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       was traditionally used to address older family members. Children were taught to use
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       to address their parents, and
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       was used until about 1950 between spouses of the higher classes. Former president Jacques Chirac and his wife Bernadette are a prominent example of the continuation of this usage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When praying,
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is nowadays often used in addressing the deity, though
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       was used in
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
        Catholic
       </a>
       prayers until the
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">
        Second Vatican Council
       </a>
       , and is still used to address the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary" title="Blessed Virgin Mary">
        Blessed Virgin Mary
       </a>
       . In Louisiana, however,
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       is always used to convey a sense of respect and reverence when praying.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Walloon, the use of which tends, in any case, to be restricted mostly to "familiar" contexts,
       <i>
        vos
       </i>
       (=
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       ) is the general usage and is considered informal and friendly.
       <i>
        Ti
       </i>
       (=
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       ), on the other hand, is considered vulgar, and its use can be taken as an expression of an aggressive attitude towards the person addressed. This influence from Walloon affects the usage of
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       in the French spoken in Belgium, though more so among people accustomed to using Walloon as their everyday language (a tiny minority, mostly in the countryside). The influence of Standard French, particularly as exercised through the
       <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">
        mass media
       </a>
       , is eroding this particularity among younger French-speakers.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the ancien régime, the use of honorific styles or their abbreviation
       <i>
        Elle
       </i>
       (lit.: she, irrespective of the gender of the addressee, as the honorifics were feminine nouns) together with the 3rd person singular was also common. See below for Italian which has kept this style.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="North_American_French">
        North American French
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: North American French">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Canadian_French" title="Canadian French">
        North American dialects of French
       </a>
       , including
       <a href="/wiki/Quebec_French" title="Quebec French">
        Quebec French
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Acadian_French" title="Acadian French">
        Acadian French
       </a>
       as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_French" title="Louisiana French">
        Louisiana French
       </a>
       , permit and expect a far broader usage of the familiar
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       than in
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_French" title="Standard French">
        Standard French
       </a>
       . There are still circumstances in which it is necessary to say
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       : in a formal interview (notably for a job) or when addressing people of very high rank (such as judges or prime ministers), senior citizens, between professors and students in universities, towards customers or new acquaintances in a formal setting. As acquaintances become familiar with one another, they may find
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       to be unnecessarily formal and may agree to return to the
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       with which they are generally more comfortable.
      </p>
      <p>
       For a number of Francophones in Canada,
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       sounds stilted or snobbish, and archaic.
       <i>
        Tu
       </i>
       is by no means restricted to intimates or social inferiors. There is however an important minority of people, often those who call for a use of standard French in Quebec, who prefer to be addressed as
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       . At
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio-Canada" title="Radio-Canada">
        Radio-Canada
       </a>
       (the public broadcaster, often considered as establishing the normative objectives of standard French in Canada), the use of
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       is widespread even among colleagues.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="African_French">
        African French
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: African French">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C3%B4te_d%27Ivoire" title="Côte d'Ivoire">
        Côte d'Ivoire
       </a>
       , local languages (such as
       <a href="/wiki/Baoul%C3%A9_language" title="Baoulé language">
        Baoulé
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dyula_language" title="Dyula language">
        Dioula
       </a>
       , etc.) do not make a distinction between informal or formal pronouns, which reflects on the local usage of French. It is thus uncommon to call an individual "vous". A waiter, shop-keeper or taxi driver can very well call a customer "vous", just like an employee towards a superior. For example, an accountant could call "tu" her direct branch manager, but will still use "vous" to address the company's CEO.
      </p>
      <p>
       Relationships between male-female are typically less formal than between people of the same gender (a female supermarket worker will more easily say "tu" towards a male customer than her male colleague).
      </p>
      <p>
       Even in formal situations (business, politics), the superiors can often be called in a familiar way by subordinates who will use affectionate terms of address such as "vieux père", "papa", "tonton", "patron", "boss" for males, "la vieille mère", "maman", "tata", "patronne" for females, instead of the standard "monsieur" and "madame". Superiors reciprocate with terms of address such as "mon fils", "mon petit", "ma chérie", "ma fille". All those terms of address typically exclude the use of "vous".
      </p>
      <p>
       The use is also conditioned to the "level" of French being spoken: using a Standard French code and/or accent (what is call "chocoter" in
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ivorian_French&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ivorian French (page does not exist)">
        Ivorian French
       </a>
       ) will prompt addresses of "vous", whereas switching to Ivorian French will typically invite a concomittant switch to "tu".
      </p>
      <p>
       Informed local people will still, most of the time, make a conscious effort to use "vous" and "monsieur"/"madame" when addressing Westerners in formal situations (unless, again, that Westerner talks Ivorian French rather than Standard French). Other groups of foreigners such as other Africans, Asians or Middle-Eastern people are less likely to enjoy that "privilege".
      </p>
      <p>
       The use of "vous", just like the use of "monsieur" and "madame", is thus restricted to very formal situations where Standard French is being used, mostly for the higher class between themselves: managers at a meeting with the CEO, representatives of different political parties, upper class people who don't know each other at a social gathering. A switching to "tu" can still happen as soon as the formal event is over (such as managers getting out of the meeting room) or just after having been introduced to each other – usually simultaneous to a switch from Standard French to
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ivorian_French&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ivorian French (page does not exist)">
        Ivorian French
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Catalan">
        Catalan
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Catalan">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       uses the singular pronouns
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       (informal) and
       <i>
        vostè
       </i>
       (formal), while
       <i>
        vosaltres
       </i>
       (informal) and
       <i>
        vostès
       </i>
       (formal) are used for two or more addressees. The form
       <i>
        vós
       </i>
       , used instead of
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       to address someone respectfully, follows the same concordance rules as the French
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       (verbs in second person plural, adjectives in singular), and
       <i>
        vostè
       </i>
       follows the same concordance rules as the Spanish
       <i>
        usted
       </i>
       (verbs in 3rd person).
       <i>
        Vostè
       </i>
       originated from
       <i>
        vostra mercè
       </i>
       as a
       <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">
        calque
       </a>
       from Spanish, and replaced the original Catalan form
       <i>
        vós
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In some dialects of Catalan,
       <i>
        vós
       </i>
       is no longer used. Other dialects have a three-way distinction
       <i>
        tu/vós/vostè
       </i>
       , where
       <i>
        vós
       </i>
       is used as a respectful form for elders and respected friends, and
       <i>
        vostè
       </i>
       for foreigners and people whom one does not know well.
       <i>
        Vostè
       </i>
       is more distant than
       <i>
        vós
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Administration uses
       <i>
        vós
       </i>
       for address to people.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish">
        Spanish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Spanish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_dialects" title="Spanish dialects">
        Spanish dialects
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Voseo" title="Voseo">
        voseo
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Spanish" title="Peninsular Spanish">
        Peninsular
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Spanish" title="Mexican Spanish">
        Mexican
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_Spanish" title="Peruvian Spanish">
        Peruvian Spanish
       </a>
       , as in
       <a href="#Italian">
        Italian
       </a>
       , an original
       <i>
        tú
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        vos
       </i>
       usage similar to
       <a href="#French">
        French
       </a>
       disappeared in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern_period" title="Early Modern period">
        Early Modern period
       </a>
       . Today,
       <i>
        tú
       </i>
       is used for informal and familiar address while the respectful form is the third-person
       <i>
        usted
       </i>
       , which can be used respectfully to anyone. Scholars argue that
       <i>
        usted
       </i>
       evolved as a contraction of the Old Spanish
       <i>
        Vuestra Merced
       </i>
       ("your mercy"), with
       <i>
        vusted
       </i>
       as a transitional form. In some cases, the title
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Don_(honorific)" title="Don (honorific)">
         Don
        </a>
       </i>
       is also employed when speaking to a respected older man, while
       <i>
        Doña
       </i>
       is used for older women.
      </p>
      <p>
       Among
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_dialects" title="Spanish dialects">
        Spanish dialects
       </a>
       , the situation is muddied by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Spain" title="Kingdom of Spain">
        kingdom
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        former empire
       </a>
       having been created during the middle of this linguistic shift. The area around
       <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">
        Colombia
       </a>
       's capital
       <a href="/wiki/Bogot%C3%A1" title="Bogotá">
        Bogotá
       </a>
       (although not the city itself) preserve an alternate respectful form
       <i>
        sumercé
       </i>
       simplified from a different contraction of
       <i>
        vuestra merced
       </i>
       . In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rioplatense" title="Rioplatense">
        Rioplatense
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        vos
       </i>
       was preserved – but as a replacement for
       <i>
        tú
       </i>
       and not as a respectful form of address; in
       <a href="/wiki/Chilean_Spanish" title="Chilean Spanish">
        Chile
       </a>
       , in Western
       <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">
        Venezuela
       </a>
       , parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">
        Colombia
       </a>
       and in
       <a href="/wiki/Central_American_Spanish" title="Central American Spanish">
        Central America
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        vos
       </i>
       is used in spoken address and
       <i>
        tú
       </i>
       is used in print and to express moderate formality, that is, it has essentially switched its function to
       <i>
        vos
       </i>
       's former role. In
       <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">
        Costa Rica
       </a>
       and part of Colombia, the use of usted is so widespread that it is not uncommon to hear parents use usted to address to their child.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the second-person plural, modern Spanish speakers in most of
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       employ
       <i>
        vosotros
       </i>
       informally and (as the third-person plural)
       <i>
        ustedes
       </i>
       to express respect. In western
       <a href="/wiki/Andalusian_Spanish" title="Andalusian Spanish">
        Andalucia
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        ustedes
       </i>
       is used in both contexts, but its verbs are conjugated in the second-person plural. Throughout the Americas and the
       <a href="/wiki/Canarian_Spanish" title="Canarian Spanish">
        Canaries
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        ustedes
       </i>
       is used in all contexts and in the third person.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese">
        Portuguese
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Portuguese">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_personal_pronouns" title="Portuguese personal pronouns">
        Portuguese personal pronouns
       </a>
      </div>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="European_Portuguese">
        European Portuguese
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: European Portuguese">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/European_Portuguese" title="European Portuguese">
        European Portuguese
       </a>
       (as well as in Africa,
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese-speaking_African_countries" title="Portuguese-speaking African countries">
        PALOP
       </a>
       , and Asia,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timor-Leste" title="Timor-Leste">
        Timor-Leste
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">
        Macau
       </a>
       ),
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       (singular "you") is commonly used as the familiar addressing pronoun, while
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       is a general form of address;
       <i>
        vocês
       </i>
       (plural both of
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       ) is used for both familiar and general. The forms
       <i>
        o senhor
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        a senhora
       </i>
       (plurals
       <i>
        os senhores
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        as senhoras
       </i>
       ) are used for more formal situations (roughly equivalent to "Mr/Sir" and "Mrs/Madam".) Similarly to some Romance languages (e.g. Italian),
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       can be omitted because the verb ending provides the necessary information. Not so much so with
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        o senhor
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        a senhora
       </i>
       because the verb ending is the same as for the third person (historically,
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       derives from
       <i>
        vossa mercê
       </i>
       ("your mercy" or "your grace") via the intermediate forms
       <i>
        vossemecê
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        vosmecê
       </i>
       ). The second person plural pronoun
       <i>
        vós
       </i>
       , from Latin
       <i>
        vos
       </i>
       , is archaic in most of the Portuguese-speaking world, but can be heard in liturgy and has a limited regional use.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Brazilian_Portuguese">
        Brazilian Portuguese
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Brazilian Portuguese">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese" title="Brazilian Portuguese">
        Brazilian Portuguese
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        vocês
       </i>
       (singular and plural "you", respectively) are used informally, while
       <i>
        o senhor
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        a senhora
       </i>
       ("Mr"/"Sir" and "Mrs"/"Madam", plurals
       <i>
        os senhores
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        as senhoras
       </i>
       ) are used in formal speech. Although now seen as archaic,
       <i>
        a senhorita
       </i>
       is used when speaking ironically, very formally or when one is demonstrating respect to a superior and it is sometimes replaced by
       <i>
        moça
       </i>
       ("Lady"). Informal terms of respect to superiors, elders or strangers are
       <i>
        Seu
       </i>
       (abbreviation of
       <i>
        senhor
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        Dona
       </i>
       (feminine of
       <i>
        Dom
       </i>
       i.e.
       <i>
        Don
       </i>
       ).
       <i>
        Moço/rapaz
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        moça
       </i>
       ("Lad"/"Young man" and "Lady") are used by seniors when addressing non-intimate youths and also as an equalizing form among strange youths.
       <i>
        Jovem
       </i>
       ("youngster") is used in the same manner by elders when addressing strange youths of both genders.
      </p>
      <p>
       On premises where the atmosphere requires extreme formality like the Senate or different courts, the protocolar forms to address dignitaries
       <i>
        Vossa Excelência
       </i>
       ("Your Excellence") and
       <i>
        Vossa Senhoria
       </i>
       ("Your Lordship/Ladyship") can still be heard. In a direct address to a judge or the president,
       <i>
        Vossa Excelência
       </i>
       must follow the vocatives
       <i>
        Meritíssimo/a
       </i>
       ("Your Honour", literally "full of merit") and
       <i>
        Sr/Sra Presidente
       </i>
       ("Mr/Mrs" President). When addressing an ecclesiastical dignitary the form
       <i>
        Vossa Reverência
       </i>
       ("Your Reverence") is used. Although
       <i>
        Vossa Senhoria
       </i>
       is regarded as protocolar, it is an equalizing form.
      </p>
      <p>
       In many parts of the geographic extension of the language e.g. most of
       <a href="/wiki/South_Region,_Brazil" title="South Region, Brazil">
        Southern
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Northeast Region, Brazil">
        Northeastern
       </a>
       Brazil, some
       <a href="/wiki/Sociolect" title="Sociolect">
        sociolects
       </a>
       of coastal São Paulo, mainly in Greater
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santos_(S%C3%A3o_Paulo)" title="Santos (São Paulo)">
        Santos
       </a>
       , colloquial
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Carioca" title="Carioca">
         carioca
        </a>
       </i>
       sociolect, mainly among the less educated and some all-
       <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">
        class
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Youth" title="Youth">
        youths
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Greater_Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Greater Rio de Janeiro">
        Greater Rio de Janeiro
       </a>
       , and in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riverense_Portu%C3%B1ol_language" title="Riverense Portuñol language">
        Uruguay
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       (singular "you" or simply "thou") is used informally, but the plural form is always
       <i>
        vocês
       </i>
       . For the overwhelmingly majority of people, the pronoun
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is commonly used with the verb conjugated as
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       (third-person singular) rather than in the traditional conjugation (second-person singular).
       <i>
        Tu
       </i>
       is somewhat familiar, even intimate, and should never be addressed to superiors, or strange elders, while
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       is much more neutral, although equalizing.
      </p>
      <p>
       The dialect that includes
       <a href="/wiki/Florian%C3%B3polis" title="Florianópolis">
        Florianópolis
       </a>
       , capital city of
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Catarina_(state)" title="Santa Catarina (state)">
        Santa Catarina
       </a>
       , as well as its shore and inner regions in the proximity like
       <a href="/wiki/Blumenau" title="Blumenau">
        Blumenau
       </a>
       , is an exception, as the use of
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is widespread, even addressing formally to an authority or to a superior. It is one of the few dialects in
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       in which second-person singular agreement is used (along with the relatively conservative dialect of the state of Maranhão).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Italian">
        Italian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Italian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Italian" title="Standard Italian">
        Standard Italian
       </a>
       the informal second-person singular pronoun is
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and the formal second-person singular pronoun is
       <i>
        Lei
       </i>
       (
       <small>
        inf.
       </small>
       "she",
       <small>
        lit.
       </small>
       "her"), always used with the third-person singular conjugation of the verb. The pronouns may be freely omitted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Despite
       <i>
        lei
       </i>
       's original meaning, modern Italian typically
       <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">
        concords
       </a>
       with the gender of the addressee when
       <i>
        lei
       </i>
       is the sentence subject; using feminine adjectives for a male addressee is not especially insulting but sounds confusing, literary or even archaic. When
       <i>
        lei
       </i>
       is an object, using feminine adjectives is normal (
       <i>
        l'ho vista
       </i>
       , i.e. "I saw you (m.)"), whereas gender concord is considered non-standard (
       <i>
        l'ho visto
       </i>
       , i.e. "I saw you (m.)).
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Lei
       </i>
       is normally used in formal settings or with strangers, although it implies a sense of distance (even coldness) similar to the
       <a href="#French">
        French
       </a>
       use of
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       . Presently Italian adults prefer to employ
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       towards strangers until around 30 years old. It is used reciprocally between adults; the usage may not be reciprocal when young people address older strangers or otherwise respected people. Students are addressed with
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       by their teachers until the end of high school with few exceptions and usually with
       <i>
        Lei
       </i>
       in universities. Students might use
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       with their teachers in elementary school, but switch to
       <i>
        Lei
       </i>
       from middle school.
       <i>
        Tu
       </i>
       is the common form of address on the Internet
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcnewsmag_lawn_2012_10-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbcnewsmag_lawn_2012-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and within some professions – such as journalism and law – as a recognition of comradeship. In the law however the
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is only used in informal settings; in the courtroom it is used only to small children, if ever any happens to appear there. The second-person plural pronoun is
       <i>
        voi
       </i>
       . Its polite counterpart was formerly
       <i>
        Loro
       </i>
       ("They"), but it is now little used outside of self-consciously formal situations such as expensive restaurants.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Voi
       </i>
       is the traditional polite form of address in
       <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_dialect" title="Tuscan dialect">
        Tuscan dialects
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">
        Dante
       </a>
       employs it in his 14th-century
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">
         Divine Comedy
        </a>
       </i>
       when showing particular respect.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Lei
       </i>
       began to replace it during the
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
        Renaissance
       </a>
       and then, under
       <a href="#Spanish">
        Spanish
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(1527)" title="Sack of Rome (1527)">
        influence
       </a>
       , it became common to contract obsequious honorifics such as "Your Lordship", "Eminence", and "Majesty", all of which are feminine third-person singular nouns in Italian (
       <i>
        Vostra Signoria
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Eminenza
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Maestà
       </i>
       ). Over the next four centuries, all three pronouns –
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Voi
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Lei
       </i>
       – were employed together to express degrees of formality and status, as displayed in
       <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Manzoni" title="Alessandro Manzoni">
        Manzoni
       </a>
       's 19th-century
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Betrothed_(Manzoni_novel)" title="The Betrothed (Manzoni novel)">
         The Betrothed
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <i>
        Voi
       </i>
       continues to be used by some speakers, particularly of
       <a href="/wiki/Regional_Italian" title="Regional Italian">
        Southern dialects
       </a>
       , as an alternative to
       <i>
        Lei
       </i>
       in polite address, but its use is increasingly uncommon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The use of
       <i>
        Voi
       </i>
       was imposed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy#Fascist_regime_(1922–1943)" title="Kingdom of Italy">
        Fascists
       </a>
       from 1938 to 1944.
       <i>
        Voi
       </i>
       still appears in comics, and in instruction books and advertisements where
       <i>
        Lei
       </i>
       would sound too distant, but in the latter case most of the time it is used directly as a plural and not as a polite singular. (An example of all three forms of address in action is the Italian
       <i>
        Lord of the Rings
       </i>
       translation: a character such as Aragorn is usually addressed as
       <i>
        lei
       </i>
       , but neither
       <i>
        lei
       </i>
       nor
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       seemed appropriate for how Samwise addresses his higher-class friend and employer Frodo; Sam calls Frodo
       <i>
        voi
       </i>
       , in consequence.)
      </p>
      <p>
       Although seldom encountered, the third person
       <i>
        la Signoria Vostra
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        la S.V.
       </i>
       ("Your Lord-" or "Ladyship") is sometimes seen in formal correspondence and invitations, as a stronger form of its descendant
       <i>
        lei
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Romanian">
        Romanian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Romanian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
        Romanian
       </a>
       <i>
        dumneavoastră
       </i>
       when used for the second-person singular formal takes plural verbs but singular adjectives, similar to French
       <i>
        vous.
       </i>
       It is used roughly in the same manner as in Continental French and shows no signs of disappearing. It is also used as a more formal
       <i>
        voi.
       </i>
       It originates from
       <i>
        domnia voastră
       </i>
       – your lordship. In the past it was used extreme rarely to nobles especially, but its sense extended to other people in the 20th century but not so common and when the communists arrived it took the actual form.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2017)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       As happens with all subject pronouns,
       <i>
        dumneavoastră
       </i>
       is often omitted from sentences, its use being implied by verbs in the second person plural form.
      </p>
      <p>
       The form
       <i>
        dumneata
       </i>
       (originating from
       <i>
        domnia ta
       </i>
       – thy lordship) is less distant than
       <i>
        dumneavoastră
       </i>
       and somewhat midway between
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        dumneavoastră.
       </i>
       The verb is conjugated, as for
       <i>
        tu,
       </i>
       in the second person singular form. Older people towards younger people and peers favor
       <i>
        dumneata
       </i>
       . Its use is gradually declining.
      </p>
      <p>
       A more colloquial form of
       <i>
        dumneata
       </i>
       is
       <i>
        mata
       </i>
       or even
       <i>
        matale
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        tălică.
       </i>
       It is more familiar than
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and is used only in some regions of Romania. It is used only with immediate family members, and is spelled and pronounced the same in all cases, similar to
       <i>
        dumneavoastră.
       </i>
       It is used with verbs in the second person singular, as is
       <i>
        tu.
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>
       The plural form is a recent borrowing. Old Romanian and Arumanian, like Classical Latin, do not have the plural form.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sicilian">
        Sicilian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Sicilian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Most dialects of Sicilian Language have utilised
       <i>
        vussìa, vossìa
       </i>
       , or
       <i>
        vassa
       </i>
       to express formality. However, due to encroachment by Italian language
       <i>
        Lei
       </i>
       has become increasingly common particularly among younger speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenic">
        Hellenic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Hellenic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_and_Hellenistic_or_Koine_Greek">
        Ancient and Hellenistic or Koine Greek
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Ancient and Hellenistic or Koine Greek">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        Ancient Greek
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        sý
       </i>
       (σύ) was the singular, and
       <i>
        hymeis
       </i>
       (ὑμεῖς) the plural, with no distinction for honorific or familiar.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" title="Paul of Tarsus">
        Paul
       </a>
       addresses King
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agrippa_II" title="Agrippa II">
        Agrippa II
       </a>
       as
       <i>
        sý
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">
        Acts
       </a>
       26:2).
      </p>
      <p>
       Later,
       <i>
        hymeís
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        hēmeís
       </i>
       (ἡμεῖς) ("we") became too close in pronunciation, and a new plural
       <i>
        seís
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        eseís
       </i>
       (σεις/εσείς) was invented, the initial
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       (ε) being a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euphony" title="Euphony">
        euphonic
       </a>
       prefix that was also extended to the singular (
       <i>
        sý/esý
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Greek">
        Modern Greek
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Modern Greek">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In Modern Greek, εσείς (
       <i>
        eseís
       </i>
       , second person plural) with second person plural verb conjugation is used as the formal counterpart of εσύ (
       <i>
        esý
       </i>
       , second person singular) when talking to strangers and elders, although in everyday life it is common to speak to strangers of your age or younger using the singular pronoun. In addition, the informal second person singular is used even with older people you are acquainted with, depending on the level of mutual familiarity.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the formal εσείς (
       <i>
        eseís
       </i>
       ) has become less common outside schools and workplaces, many people often do not know which form to use (because using a formal version might sound too snobbish even to an elder and using the informal version might sound inappropriate to some strangers) and thus prefer to replace verbs with nouns (avoiding the dilemma) until enough information on the counterpart's intentions is gathered in order to choose between formal or informal second person pronoun and verb conjugation. A good rule of thumb is that singular accompanies first names and plural accompanies surnames with title (Mr, Mrs, etc.). Exceptions are rare, for example younger schoolchildren may address their teacher in the plural, title and
       <i>
        first name
       </i>
       , or an officer may address a soldier in the singular and surname. The sequence singular-title-surname is a faux pas that can often indicate lack of education, of good manners, or of both.
      </p>
      <p>
       The modern
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_custom" title="Social custom">
        social custom
       </a>
       when using the
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek language
       </a>
       in Greece is to ask the other person "may we speak in the singular?" in which the other person is expected to answer "yes" and afterwards the discussion continues using the informal
       <i>
        εσύ
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        esý
       </i>
       ); it is unthinkable for the other person to answer "no" or show preference for plural forms, and for this reason one should not even ask this question to a person of high status, such as a professional. Therefore, asking this question can itself be considered a form of disrespect in some social situations. Likewise, not asking this question and simply using the singular without prior explicit or implicit agreement would also be considered disrespectful in various social contingencies. In other cases, even using the formal plural (without a question) could also be considered offensive. A person being inappropriately addressed in the singular will often indicate their displeasure by insisting on responding in the plural, in a display of irony that may or may not be evident to the other party. A similar social custom exists with the words
       <i>
        κύριε
       </i>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mr" title="Mr">
        Mr
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Sir" title="Sir">
        Sir
       </a>
       ) and
       <i>
        κυρία
       </i>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mrs" title="Mrs">
        Mrs
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Madam" title="Madam">
        Madam
       </a>
       ), which can show both respect and a form of "mock respect" essentially communicating disapproval, often depending on the voice intonation and the social situation. Overall, the distinction between formal and informal forms of address and when to use each can be quite subtle and not easily discernible by a non-native speaker.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_Greek" title="Cypriot Greek">
        Cypriot Greek
       </a>
       traditionally had no T–V distinction, with even persons of very high social status addressed in the singular, usually together with an honorific or title such as
       <i>
        δάσκαλε
       </i>
       ("teacher", mainly for priests) or
       <i>
        μάστρε
       </i>
       (literally "master", loosely "sir"). Even today, the singular form is used much more frequently in
       <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
        Cyprus
       </a>
       compared to Greece, although this is changing under the influence of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Modern_Greek" title="Standard Modern Greek">
        Standard Modern Greek
       </a>
       . The plural form is now expected in a formal setting.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Celtic">
        Celtic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Celtic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Scottish_Gaelic">
        Scottish Gaelic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Scottish Gaelic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_language" title="Scottish Gaelic language">
        Scottish Gaelic
       </a>
       , the informal form of the second-person singular is
       <i>
        thu
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       (emphatic:
       <i>
        thusa
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        tusa
       </i>
       ), used when addressing a person the speaker knows well, or when addressing a person younger or relatively the same age as the speaker. When addressing a superior, an elder, or a stranger, or in conducting business, the form
       <i>
        sibh
       </i>
       (emphatic:
       <i>
        sibhse
       </i>
       ) is used. (
       <i>
        Sibh
       </i>
       is also the second person plural). This distinction carries over into
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjugated_preposition" title="Conjugated preposition">
        prepositional pronouns
       </a>
       : for instance,
       <i>
        agad
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        agaibh
       </i>
       (at you),
       <i>
        riut
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ribh
       </i>
       (against you),
       <i>
        romhad
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        romhaibh
       </i>
       (before you), etc., and into
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Possessive_pronoun" title="Possessive pronoun">
        possessive pronouns
       </a>
       <i>
        do
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ur
       </i>
       (your).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Irish">
        Irish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Irish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In Irish, the use of
       <i>
        sibh
       </i>
       as an address to one person has died out, and
       <i>
        tú
       </i>
       is preferred. Formerly,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">
        Roman Catholic
       </a>
       priests were addressed with the plural form
       <i>
        sibh
       </i>
       , especially in Ulster, due to the possibility that the priest may be carrying the
       <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">
        Eucharist
       </a>
       on his person—belief in the
       <a href="/wiki/Real_presence_of_Christ_in_the_Eucharist" title="Real presence of Christ in the Eucharist">
        real presence of Christ in the Eucharist
       </a>
       would require the use of the plural.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Welsh.2C_Cornish_and_Breton">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Welsh,_Cornish_and_Breton">
        Welsh, Cornish and Breton
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Welsh, Cornish and Breton">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Modern
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
        Welsh
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
        Cornish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
        Breton
       </a>
       all retain a T–V distinction to varying degrees.
      </p>
      <p>
       In spoken Welsh, the plural pronoun
       <i>
        chi
       </i>
       is used when speaking to strangers, elders or superiors, while
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       (or
       <i>
        chdi
       </i>
       in some parts of the North) is used with friends, close family, animals and children.
       <i>
        Ti
       </i>
       is also the form used when addressing God. Nonetheless, the use of
       <i>
        chi
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       varies between families and regions, but those guidelines are generally observed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JonesWelsh_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-JonesWelsh-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Chwi
       </i>
       is an alternative to
       <i>
        chi
       </i>
       found in very formal literary language. Alongside the usages explained above,
       <a href="/wiki/Silent_Generation" title="Silent Generation">
        those born before 1945
       </a>
       would, in their youth, use
       <i>
        chi
       </i>
       with a girl of about the same age.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JonesWelsh_53-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-JonesWelsh-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Similarly to
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       , the third person singular is used by some speakers in the former
       <a href="/wiki/Dyfed" title="Dyfed">
        Dyfed
       </a>
       region of west Wales; it appears, however, that the pronoun used – between either
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        fe
       </i>
       (masculine) and
       <i>
        hi
       </i>
       (feminine) – depends on the gender of the listener.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JonesWelsh_53-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-JonesWelsh-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A similar distinction exists between Cornish singular
       <i>
        ty / chy
       </i>
       and plural
       <i>
        hwi / whi
       </i>
       . The singular form is used when talking to friends, family, animals and children, and the plural form is used to talk to a group of people, or when being especially polite to one person.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Breton the second person plural
       <i>
