        c'hwi
       </i>
       is used as a polite form when addressing a single person and the singular
       <i>
        te
       </i>
       is reserved for informal situations. However, in a large area of central Brittany the singular form has been entirely replaced by
       <i>
        c'hwi
       </i>
       , as in English.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Balto-Slavic">
        Balto-Slavic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Balto-Slavic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span id="Russian_and_Ukrainian_.28mainly_Eastern.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_and_Ukrainian_(mainly_Eastern)">
        Russian and Ukrainian (mainly Eastern)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Russian and Ukrainian (mainly Eastern)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Modern
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">
        Russian
       </a>
       distinguishes between the familiar
       <i>
        <b>
         ty
        </b>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        ты
       </i>
       ) and the respectful
       <i>
        <b>
         vy
        </b>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        вы
       </i>
       ), the latter also being the plural of both forms. (Respectful
       <i>
        Vy
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Вы
       </i>
       ) may be capitalised in written correspondence, while plural
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       is not.) The distinction appeared relatively recently and began to gain currency among the educated classes in the 18th century through French influence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popov_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-popov-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Generally,
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       is used among friends and relatives, but the usage depends not only on the closeness of the relationship but also on age and the formality of the situation (e.g., work meeting vs. a party). Children always use
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       to address each other and are addressed in this way by adults but are taught to address adults with
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       . Younger adults typically also address older adults outside the family as
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       regardless of intimacy, and may be addressed as
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       in return. When talking to each other young people often start with the formal
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       but may transition to
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       very quickly in an informal situation. Among older people,
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       is often reserved for closer acquaintances. Unless there is a substantial difference in age, the choice of the form is symmetric: if A uses
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       to address B, then B also uses
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       to address A. While people may transition quickly from
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       , such transition presumes mutual agreement. Use of
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       without consent of the other person is likely to be viewed as poor conduct or even as an insult (or, in the case of opposite-sexed people, overly flirtatious), particularly if the other party maintains using
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Historically, the rules used to be more
       <a href="/wiki/Estates_of_the_realm" title="Estates of the realm">
        class
       </a>
       -specific: as late as at the end of the 19th century, it was accepted in some circles (in aristocracy and especially gentry) that
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       was to be used also between friends, between husband and wife, and when addressing one's parents (but not one's children), all of which situations today would strongly call for using
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       . Meanwhile, up to this day, common people, especially those living in rural areas, hardly ever use the polite
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popov_54-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-popov-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Russian speakers online uphold the distinction and mainly use
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       for strangers, although in the earlier days of internet it was more common and expected to use
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       to address everyone.
      </p>
      <p>
       The choice between
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       is closely related to, yet sometimes different from, the choice of the addressing format – that is, the selection from the first name,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patronymics" title="Patronymics">
        patronymics
       </a>
       , last name, and the title to be used when addressing the person. Normally,
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       is associated with the informal addressing by first name only (or, even more informally, by the patronymic only), whereas
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       is associated with the more formal addressing format of using the first name together with patronymics (roughly analogous to "title followed by last name" in English) or the last name together with a title (the last name is almost never used together with either of the other two names to
       <i>
        address
       </i>
       someone, although such combinations are routinely used to
       <i>
        introduce
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        mention
       </i>
       someone). However, nowadays,
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       can also be employed while addressing by first name only.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Ukrainian, the present practice is essentially the same as in Russian, historically this was primarily in the Eastern, Russian-ruled part of Ukraine. Until about 1945, due to Polish influence, the practices in the former Galicia and Volyn' regions, tended to more closely resemble the Polish practices, as described below. But since those areas became annexed to the Soviet Union, the East Ukrainian and Russian practices have become prevalent all over Ukraine, with the
       <i>
        panstvo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        proshu pana
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        proshu pani
       </i>
       , etc. forms only being preserved in the emigre diaspora.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Serbo-Croatian">
        Serbo-Croatian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Serbo-Croatian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In all standard forms of
       <a href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian" title="Serbo-Croatian">
        Serbo-Croatian
       </a>
       , i.e.
       <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">
        Serbian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">
        Croatian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Montenegrin_language" title="Montenegrin language">
        Montenegrin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_language" title="Bosnian language">
        Bosnian
       </a>
       , the use of
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       is limited to friends and family, and used among children. In any formal use,
       <i>
        vi
       </i>
       , the second-person plural, is used only;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       can be used among peers in a workplace but is rare in official documents. It is a common misconception, even among native speakers, that
       <i>
        vi
       </i>
       is always capitalized when used in formal tone;
       <i>
        Vi
       </i>
       is capitalized only in direct personal correspondence between two persons.
      </p>
      <p>
       With the polite
       <i>
        vi
       </i>
       , masculine plural (in participles and adjectives) is used regardless of the sex of the person addressed.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Bulgarian">
        Bulgarian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Bulgarian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">
        Bulgarian
       </a>
       distinguishes between familiar
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        ти
       </i>
       ) and respectful
       <i>
        Vie
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Вие
       </i>
       ).
       <i>
        Ti
       </i>
       is always singular and implies familiarity.
       <i>
        Vie
       </i>
       , the plural of
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       , also functions as the formal singular.
      </p>
      <p>
       In addressing more than one person, the plural
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       is always used. For example, "
       <i>
        Вие
       </i>
       двамата напуснете, моля!" means "
       <i>
        You
       </i>
       two leave, please!"). Here, although
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       both means
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       can not be used.
      </p>
      <p>
       When addressing a single person, if the people talking are acquainted then singular
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       is used, otherwise plural
       <i>
        Vie
       </i>
       should be used. Sometimes people start a new acquaintance straightforwardly with singular
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       , but generally this is considered offensive, rude, or simply impolite. Children are taught to always use
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       between themselves, but
       <i>
        Vie
       </i>
       for addressing more than one child or an unknown adult.
      </p>
      <p>
       The grammatically correct spelling of the singular word
       <i>
        Vie
       </i>
       is always with a capital letter, whether being the first word in a sentence or not. For example, the sentence "But
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       are wrong!", if spelled (in Bulgarian) "Но
       <i>
        Вие
       </i>
       грешите!" (the word
       <i>
        Вие
       </i>
       with capital
       <i>
        В
       </i>
       ), it would convey that the speaker is addressing an individual person with a plural, because he/she wants to express a polite, official manner; if spelt "Но
       <i>
        вие
       </i>
       грешите!" (the second possible Bulgarian translation of "But
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       are wrong!"), it would then mean that someone is talking to several persons.
      </p>
      <p>
       Generally,
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       is used among friends and relatives. When talking to each other, young people often start with the formal
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       but quickly transition to
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       in an informal situation. Unless there is a substantial difference in social situation (e.g. a teacher and a student), the choice of the form is symmetric: if A. uses
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       to address B., then B. also uses
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       to address A. While people may transition quickly from
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       , such transition presumes mutual agreement. There is a recent trend not to use the formal
       <i>
        Vie
       </i>
       at all, but this can lead to awkward situations.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Macedonian">
        Macedonian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Macedonian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_language" title="Macedonian language">
        Macedonian
       </a>
       distinguishes between familiar
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       (ти) and respectful
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       (вие) – which is also the plural of both forms, used to address a pair or group. (Respectful
       <i>
        Vie
       </i>
       may be capitalized, while plural
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       is not.) Generally,
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       is used among friends and relatives, but the usage depends not only on the closeness of the relationship but also on age and the formality of the situation (e.g., work meeting vs. a party). Children always use
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       to address each other and are addressed in this way by adults, but are taught to address adults with
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       . Younger adults typically also address older adults outside the family as
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       regardless of intimacy, and may be addressed as
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       in return. When talking to each other young people often start with the formal
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       , but may transit to
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       very quickly in an informal situation. Among older people,
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       is often reserved for closer acquaintances. Unless there is a substantial difference in age, the choice of the form is symmetric: if A uses
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       to address B, then B also uses
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       to address A. While people may transit quickly from
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       , such transition presumes mutual agreement. Use of
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       without consent of the other person is likely to be viewed as poor conduct or even as an insult, particularly if the other party maintains using
       <i>
        vie
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Polish">
        Polish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Polish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Polish_name#Formal_and_informal_use" title="Polish name">
        Polish name § Formal and informal use
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Honorifics_(linguistics)#Polish" title="Honorifics (linguistics)">
        Polish honorifics
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Polish uses as formal forms the words
       <i>
        pan
       </i>
       (meaning "mister" or "gentleman") and
       <i>
        pani
       </i>
       ("lady"), and in the plural
       <i>
        panowie
       </i>
       ("gentlemen") and
       <i>
        panie
       </i>
       ("ladies") respectively,
       <i>
        państwo
       </i>
       being used for mixed groups (originally a neutral noun, meaning roughly "lordship", but also, and until today, "state").
       <i>
        Państwo
       </i>
       is used with the plural, like
       <i>
        panowie
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        panie
       </i>
       . Because of their character as nouns (and not pronouns) these words are used with the third person: For example, the familiar
       <i>
        Chcesz pić
       </i>
       ("You want to drink") becomes
       <i>
        Pan chce pić
       </i>
       (literally "The gentleman wants to drink").
      </p>
      <p>
       Further,
       <i>
        pan
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        pani
       </i>
       can be combined with the first name, the last name and with titles like "President", "Professor", "Doctor", "Editor" and others (
       <i>
        Pan Prezydent
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        pani profesor
       </i>
       etc.; using these titles is considered necessary); using both (
       <i>
        Pan Prezydent Kowalski
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        pani profesor Nowak
       </i>
       ) is considered more polite or, in some context, even submissive. Addressing a present person with the last name is only usual in court or in other affairs, where government authority is involved, and generally considered impolite or condescending.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When addressing someone, all these forms always require the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vocative" title="Vocative">
        vocative
       </a>
       case, which is otherwise optional (for example
       <i>
        panie Kowalski
       </i>
       ("Mr Kowalski!"),
       <i>
        pani Joanno
       </i>
       ("Mrs Joanna!"),
       <i>
        panie profesorze
       </i>
       ("professor!")). For
       <i>
        pan
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        pani
       </i>
       etc. alone,
       <i>
        proszę
       </i>
       +
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genitive" title="Genitive">
        genitive
       </a>
       is used instead of vocative:
       <i>
        proszę pana
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        proszę pani
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        proszę panów
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        proszę pań
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        proszę państwa
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Western Ukrainian, as spoken in Galicia and Volyn' until about 1945, tended to copy the Polish forms such as
       <i>
        proshu pana
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        proshu pani
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        shanovne panstvo
       </i>
       , etc., but this was primarily used by Ukrainians to address social superiors. Among social equals, the
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       form was mainly used by Ukrainians, but close friends and family were addressed as
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       , comparable to the practice in Russia and Poland.
      </p>
      <p>
       A unique practice among both Poles and Western Ukrainians is addressing a lawyer as
       <i>
        pan Mecenas
       </i>
       , meaning "Mr. Philanthropist", derived from the name of an ancient Greek king known for his generosity. As Ukrainians had a married priesthood, a priest's wife was addressed as
       <i>
        pani Dobrodijka
       </i>
       (Mrs. good-deed-doer), while a priest was addressed as
       <i>
        pan Otec
        <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
         '
        </span>
       </i>
       (Mr. Father), the Polish-derived word
       <i>
        ksjondz
        <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
         '
        </span>
       </i>
       was considered somewhat slangish and disrespectful when speaking to a Ukrainian priest.
      </p>
      <p>
       The V-forms are capitalized only in actual letters (or e-mails), where the T-forms
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        wy
       </i>
       are also capitalized.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Plural
       <i>
        wy
       </i>
       is also used as V-form in dialects, for example
       <i>
        Matko, co wy jecie?
       </i>
       ("Mother, what are you eating?").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is also associated with stereotypical communists and officials, especially of
       <a href="/wiki/Polish_People%27s_Republic" title="Polish People's Republic">
        communist era in Poland
       </a>
       . Among Ukrainians,
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       is the common form used to address social equals who are not close friends or family.
      </p>
      <p>
       Besides, other forms can be sometimes used like
       <i>
        pan
       </i>
       in third person when talking to older family members (
       <i>
        Niech mama powie
       </i>
       , "Say, mother"),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to clergy (
       <i>
        Tak, dobrze ksiądz trafił
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "Yes, you, priest, are in right place") or to other people in less formal or semi-formal situations, e.g. polite quarrel or dispute (
       <i>
        Zatem – proszę kolegi – niech kolega się trochę douczy, a potem poucza innych
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "Also, dear friend, learn more and then instruct others").
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Slovene">
        Slovene
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Slovene">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jozef_Nahtigal_tombstone_in_Dobrova_Slovenia.jpg">

        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jozef_Nahtigal_tombstone_in_Dobrova_Slovenia.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Tombstone of
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jo%C5%BEef_Nahtigal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jožef Nahtigal (page does not exist)">
          Jožef Nahtigal
         </a>
         in
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dobrova,_Dobrova_%E2%80%93_Polhov_Gradec" title="Dobrova, Dobrova – Polhov Gradec">
          Dobrova
         </a>
         with archaic Slovene
         <i>
          onikanje
         </i>
         in indirect reference. Literal translation "Here lie [
         <i>
          počivajo
         </i>
         ] the honorable Jožef Nahtigal … they were born [
         <i>
          rojeni
         </i>
         ] … they died [
         <i>
          umerli
         </i>
         ] … God grant them [
         <i>
          jim
         </i>
         ] eternal peace and rest."
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slovenian_language" title="Slovenian language">
        Slovenian
       </a>
       , although informal address using the 2nd person singular
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       form (known as
       <i>
        tikanje
       </i>
       ) is officially limited to friends and family, talk among children, and addressing animals, it is increasingly used instead of its polite or formal counterpart using the 2nd person plural
       <i>
        vi
       </i>
       form (known as
       <i>
        vikanje
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       There is an additional nonstandard but widespread use of a singular participle combined with a plural auxiliary verb (known as
       <i>
        polvikanje
       </i>
       ) that also reveals the gender of the person and is used in somewhat less formal situations:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         Vi ga niste videli.
        </i>
        ('You did not see him': both the auxiliary verb
        <i>
         niste
        </i>
        and the participle
        <i>
         videli
        </i>
        are plural masculine.)
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Vi ga niste videl/videla.
        </i>
        ('You did not see him': the auxiliary verb
        <i>
         niste
        </i>
        is plural but the participle
        <i>
         videl/videla
        </i>
        is singular masculine/feminine.)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The use of the 3rd person plural
       <i>
        oni
       </i>
       form (known as
       <i>
        onikanje
       </i>
       in both direct address and indirect reference) as an ultra-polite form is now archaic or dialectal; it is associated with servant-master relationships in older literature, the child-parent relationship in certain conservative rural communities, and in general with relationships with people of highest respect (parents, clergy, royalty).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Czech">
        Czech
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Czech">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           needs additional citations for
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            verification
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit">
            improve this article
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
            adding citations to reliable sources
           </a>
           . Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (July 2011)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Czech_verb" title="Czech verb">
        Czech verb
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">
        Czech
       </a>
       , there are three levels of formality. The most formal is using the second person plural verb forms (V form) with the surname or title of the addressed person, usual between strangers or people in a professional relationship. The second common form is made by using the second person singular verb forms (T form) together with the given name of the other person, used between friends and in certain social groups (students etc.). The third form, which is rather less common, is using the V form in combination with the given name. It may be used by a teacher when addressing a student (especially at the secondary school level), by a boss addressing his secretary, or in other relationships where a certain degree of familiarity has developed, but has not superseded some level of mutually acknowledged respect or distance. This form of address is usually asymmetrical (the perceived social superior uses V form in combination with the first name, the perceived social inferior using V form and the surname or honorific), less often symmetrical. Using the singular verb forms together with the surname or title is considered very rude. Where a stranger introduces himself with title (like
       <i>
        inženýr Novák
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        doktor Svoboda
       </i>
       ), it is considered more polite to address him using the V form in combination with his title (always preceded by the honorific
       <i>
        paní
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        pane
       </i>
       , i.e. Mr/Madam), rather than his surname. However, it is considered poor manners to address somebody with his title in combination with the T form.
      </p>
      <p>
       Traditionally, use of the informal form was limited for relatives, very close friends, and for children. During the second half of the 20th century, use of the informal form grew significantly among coworkers, youth and members of organisations and groups. The formal form is always used in official documents and when dealing with a stranger (especially an older one) as a sign of respect. 2nd-person pronouns (
       <i>
        Ty, Tvůj, Vy, Váš
       </i>
       ) are often capitalized in letters, advertisement, etc. The capitalization is optional and is slowly becoming obsolete. A variant of the formal form modeled after German
       <i>
        Sie
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Oni/oni
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Jejich/jejich
       </i>
       , verb
       <i>
        onikat
       </i>
       ) was frequently used during the 19th century but has since disappeared. This form is also associated with Czech
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jew" title="Jew">
        Jewish
       </a>
       community before
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">
        Second World War
       </a>
       , and still appears very often in
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_humour" title="Jewish humour">
        Jewish humour
       </a>
       as sign of local colour. Sometimes it is used as
       <a href="/wiki/Irony" title="Irony">
        irony
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the Internet age, where people communicate under nicknames or pseudonymes and almost solely in informal way, capitalizing (
       <i>
        ty/Ty
       </i>
       mirroring English
       <i>
        you/You
       </i>
       ) is used to emphasise respect, or simply presence of respect. (
       <i>
        Ty
       </i>
       = friends, honored acquaintance, strangers
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       = basic form,
       <i>
        vy/Vy
       </i>
       = most formal, used to create distance or express contempt, very rude if not sufficiently advocated, often used as insult itself).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In grammar, plural forms are used in personal and possessive
       <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">
        pronouns
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       – you,
       <i>
        váš
       </i>
       – your) and in verbs, but not in
       <a href="/wiki/Participle" title="Participle">
        participles
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">
        adjectives
       </a>
       , they are used in singular forms (when addressing a single person). This differs from some other
       <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">
        Slavic languages
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Slovak_language" title="Slovak language">
        Slovak
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">
        Russian
       </a>
       , etc.)
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         One person
         <br/>
         informal
         <br/>
         (tykání)
        </th>
        <th>
         One person
         <br/>
         formal
         <br/>
         (vykání)
        </th>
        <th>
         More people
         <br/>
         (both formal
         <br/>
         and informal)
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         ty děláš
        </td>
        <td>
         vy děláte
        </td>
        <td>
         vy děláte
        </td>
        <td>
         you do
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         dělal jsi
        </td>
        <td>
         dělal jste
        </td>
        <td>
         dělali jste
        </td>
        <td>
         you did
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         jsi hodný
        </td>
        <td>
         jste hodný
        </td>
        <td>
         jste hodní
        </td>
        <td>
         you are kind
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         byl jsi přijat
        </td>
        <td>
         byl jste přijat
        </td>
        <td>
         byli jste přijati
        </td>
        <td>
         you were accepted
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Greetings are also connected with T–V distinction. Formal
       <i>
        dobrý den
       </i>
       (good day) and
       <i>
        na shledanou
       </i>
       (good-bye) are used with formal
       <i>
        vy
       </i>
       , while
       <i>
        ahoj, nazdar, čau
       </i>
       (meaning both
       <i>
        hello, hi
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        bye
       </i>
       ) are informal and used with
       <i>
        ty
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lithuanian">
        Lithuanian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Lithuanian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">
        Lithuanian
       </a>
       , historically, aside from familiar
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and respectful
       <i>
        jūs
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Jūs
       </i>
       , also used to express plural, there was a special form
       <i>
        tamsta
       </i>
       , mostly referred to in third person singular (although referring in second person singular is also not uncommon). This form was used to communicate with a stranger who has not earned particular respect (a beggar, for example). Modern Lithuanian Dictionary describes
       <i>
        tamsta
       </i>
       as a polite form of second singular person
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       making its meaning somewhere in the middle between informal
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and formal
       <i>
        jūs
       </i>
       . Through the Soviet occupation period, however, this form was mostly replaced by standard neutral form
       <i>
        drauge
       </i>
       (the
       <a href="/wiki/Vocative_case" title="Vocative case">
        vocative case
       </a>
       for
       <i>
        draugas
       </i>
       , "
       <a href="/wiki/Comrade" title="Comrade">
        comrade
       </a>
       ", the latter being the standard formal form of addressing in all languages of the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       used in all situations, from "comrade
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">
        Stalin
       </a>
       " to "comrade student"), and by now
       <i>
        tamsta
       </i>
       is used sparsely. A common way of addressing people whom one doesn't know well is also
       <i>
        Ponas
       </i>
       (m) and
       <i>
        Ponia
       </i>
       (f), from Polish forms of address
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pan#Polish" title="wikt:pan">
         pan
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pani#Polish" title="wikt:pani">
         pani
        </a>
       </i>
       , respectively.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Indic">
        Indic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Indic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hindi_and_Urdu">
        Hindi and Urdu
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Hindi and Urdu">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In the standard forms of both
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Hindi" title="Standard Hindi">
        Hindi
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Urdu" title="Standard Urdu">
        Urdu
       </a>
       there are three levels of honorifics:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         आप آپ
        </b>
        <i>
         āp
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [aːp]
        </span>
        : Formal and respectable form for
        <i>
         you
        </i>
        . Used in all formal settings and speaking to persons who are senior in job or age. No difference between the singular and the plural; plural reference can, however, be indicated by the use of "you people" (आप लोग آپ لوگ
        <i>
         āp log
        </i>
        ) or "you all" (आप सब آپ سب
        <i>
         āp sab
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         तुम تُم
        </b>
        <i>
         tum
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [tʊm]
        </span>
        : Informal form of
        <i>
         you
        </i>
        . Used in all informal settings and speaking to persons who are junior in job or age. No difference between the singular and the plural; plural reference can, however, be indicated by the use of "you people" (तुम लोग تُم لوگ
        <i>
         tum log
        </i>
        ) or "you all" (तुम सब تُم سب
        <i>
         tum sab
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         तू تُو
        </b>
        <i>
         tū
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [tuː]
        </span>
        : Extremely informal form of
        <i>
         you
        </i>
        . Strictly singular, its plural form would be तुम تُم
        <i>
         tum
        </i>
        . Inappropriate use of this form – i.e. other than in addressing children, very close friends, or in poetic language (either with God or with lovers) – risks being perceived as offensive in Pakistan or India.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Bengali">
        Bengali
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Bengali">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">
        Bengali
       </a>
       has three levels of formality in its pronouns; the most neutral forms of address among closer members of a family are তুমি
       <i>
        tumi
       </i>
       and তোমরা
       <i>
        tomra
       </i>
       (plural). These two pronouns are also typically used when speaking to children, or to younger members of the extended family. তুমি
       <i>
        tumi
       </i>
       is also used when addressing God. When speaking with adults outside the family, or with senior members of the extended family, the pronouns আপনি
       <i>
        apni
       </i>
       and আপনারা
       <i>
        apnara
       </i>
       (plural) are used. This is also true in advertisements and public announcements. A third set of pronouns, তুই
       <i>
        tui
       </i>
       and তোরা
       <i>
        tora
       </i>
       (plural), is reserved for use between very close friends, and by extension, between relatives who share a bond not unlike a close friendship. It is also used when addressing people presumed to be of "inferior" social status; this latter use is occasionally used when speaking to housemaids, rickshaw-pullers, and other service workers, although this use is considered offensive.
      </p>
      <p>
       The situations in which these different pronouns can be used vary considerably depending on many social factors. In some families, children may address their parents with আপনি
       <i>
        apni
       </i>
       and আপনারা
       <i>
        apnara
       </i>
       , although this is becoming increasingly rare. Some adults alternate between all three pronoun levels when speaking to children, normally choosing তুমি
       <i>
        tumi
       </i>
       and তোমরা
       <i>
        tomra
       </i>
       , but also often choosing তুই
       <i>
        tui
       </i>
       and তোরা
       <i>
        tora
       </i>
       to indicate closeness, or আপনি
       <i>
        apni
       </i>
       or আপনারা
       <i>
        apnara
       </i>
       in a joking manner. Additionally, Bengalis vary in which pronoun they use when addressing servants in the home; some may use আপনি
       <i>
        apni
       </i>
       and আপনারা
       <i>
        apnara
       </i>
       to indicate respect for an adult outside the family, while others may use তুমি
       <i>
        tumi
       </i>
       and তোমরা
       <i>
        tomra
       </i>
       to indicate either inclusion into the family or to indicate somewhat less honorable status. Others may even use তুই
       <i>
        tui
       </i>
       and তোরা
       <i>
        tora
       </i>
       to indicate inferior status.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Finno-Ugric">
        Finno-Ugric
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Finno-Ugric">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Finnish">
        Finnish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Finnish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       , today the use of the informal singular form of address (
       <i>
        sinä
       </i>
       ) is widespread in all social circles, even among strangers and in business situations. The use of formal address has not disappeared however, and persists in situations involving customer service (especially if the customer is clearly older than the person serving them) and in general in addressing the elderly or in situations where strict adherence to form is expected, such as in the military. An increase in the use of formal address has been reported in recent years, whereby some people are choosing to use the formal form more often.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       . As the use of the form conveys formal recognition of the addressee's status and, more correctly, of polite social distance, the formal form might also occasionally be used jeeringly or to protest the addressee's snobbery. A native speaker may also switch to formal form when speaking in anger, as an attempt to remain civil. Advertisements, instructions and other formal messages are mostly in informal singular form (
       <i>
        sinä
       </i>
       and its conjugations), but the use of formal forms has increased in recent years. For example, as the tax authorities tend to become more informal, in contrast the social security system is reverting to using the formal form.
      </p>
      <p>
       The same forms, such as the pronoun
       <i>
        te
       </i>
       , are used for formal singular and for both formal and informal plural.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Finnish the number is expressed in pronouns (
       <i>
        sinä
       </i>
       for second person singular, or
       <i>
        te
       </i>
       for second person plural), verb inflections, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Possessive_suffix" title="Possessive suffix">
        possessive suffixes
       </a>
       . Almost all of these elements follow the grammar of the second person plural also in the formal singular form. For example, polite
       <i>
        Voisitteko (te) siirtää autonne
       </i>
       vs. informal
       <i>
        Voisitko (sinä) siirtää autosi
       </i>
       , "Could you move your car, (please)?". Each of the person markers are modified:
       <i>
        -t-
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        -tte-
       </i>
       (verb person),
       <i>
        sinä
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        te
       </i>
       (pronoun),
       <i>
        -si
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        -nne
       </i>
       (possessive suffix).
      </p>
      <p>
       As a few examples of this could be mentioned the way
       <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">
        imperatives
       </a>
       are expressed:
       <i>
        Menkää!
       </i>
       "Go!" (plural), vs.
       <i>
        Mene!
       </i>
       "Go!" (singular), and the usage of the plural suffix
       <i>
        -nne
       </i>
       "your" instead of the singular
       <i>
        -si
       </i>
       "your".
      </p>
      <p>
       There is number agreement in Finnish, thus you say
       <i>
        sinä olet
       </i>
       "you are" (singular), but
       <i>
        te olette
       </i>
       "you are" (plural). However, this does not extend to words describing the addressee, which are in the singular, e.g.
       <i>
        oletteko te lääkäri?
       </i>
       "are you doctor?" (plural, plural, singular)
      </p>
      <p>
       A common error, nowadays often made even by native speakers unused to the formal forms, is to use the plural form of the main verb in the
       <a href="/wiki/Perfect_(grammar)" title="Perfect (grammar)">
        perfect
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pluperfect" title="Pluperfect">
        pluperfect
       </a>
       constructions. The main verb should be in the singular when addressing one person in the formal plural:
       <i>
        Oletteko kuullut?
       </i>
       instead of
       <i>
        *Oletteko kuulleet?
       </i>
       "Have you heard?"
      </p>
      <p>
       Sometimes the third person is used as a polite form of address, after the
       <a href="/wiki/T%E2%80%93V_distinction#Swedish" title="T–V distinction">
        Swedish model
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Mitä rouvalle saisi olla?
       </i>
       "What would madam like to have?" This is far less common in the Eastern parts of Finland, influenced less by the Swedish language and all in all a declining habit. The
       <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">
        passive voice
       </a>
       may be used to circumvent the choice of the correct form of address. In another meaning, the passive voice is also the equivalent of the English patronizing
       <i>
        we
       </i>
       as in
       <i>
        Kuinkas tänään voidaan?
       </i>
       "How are we feeling today?"
      </p>
      <p>
       Finnish language includes the verbs for calling one with informal singular or formal plural:
       <i>
        sinutella
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        teititellä
       </i>
       , respectively.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the Bible and in the
       <a href="/wiki/Kalevala" title="Kalevala">
        Kalevala
       </a>
       , only the "informal" singular is used in all cases.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Estonian">
        Estonian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Estonian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">
        Estonian
       </a>
       is a language with T–V distinction, second person plural (
       <i>
        teie
       </i>
       ) is used instead of second person singular (
       <i>
        sina
       </i>
       ) as a means of expressing politeness or formal speech.
       <i>
        Sina
       </i>
       is the familiar form of address used with family, friends, and minors. The distinction is still much more widely used and more rigid than in closely related Finnish language.
      </p>
      <p>
       Similar to the French language
       <i>
        vouvoyer
       </i>
       , the verb
       <i>
        teietama
       </i>
       is used, and
       <i>
        teie
       </i>
       is used when addressing a (new) customer or a patient, or when talking to a person in his/her function. In
       <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_organization" title="Hierarchical organization">
        hierarchical organizations
       </a>
       , like large businesses or armies,
       <i>
        sina
       </i>
       is used between members of a same rank/level while
       <i>
        teie
       </i>
       is used between members of different ranks.
       <i>
        Sina
       </i>
       (the verb
       <i>
        sinatama
       </i>
       is also used) is used with relatives, friends, when addressing children and with close colleagues. Borderline situations, such as distant relatives, young adults, customers in rental shops or new colleagues, sometimes still present difficulties.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hungarian">
        Hungarian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Hungarian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">
        Hungarian
       </a>
       provides numerous, often subtle means of T–V distinction:
      </p>
      <p>
       The use of the second-person conjugation with the pronoun
       <i>
        te
       </i>
       (plural
       <i>
        ti
       </i>
       ) is the most informal mode. As in many other European languages, it is used within families, among children, lovers, close friends, (nowadays often) among coworkers, and in some communities, suggesting an idea of brotherhood. Adults unilaterally address children this way, and it is the form used in addressing God and other Christian figures (such as Jesus Christ or the Blessed Virgin), animals, and objects or ideas. Sociologically, the use of this form is widening. Whereas traditionally the switch to
       <i>
        te
       </i>
       is often a symbolic milestone between people, sometimes sealed by drinking a glass of wine together (
       <i>
        pertu
       </i>
       ), today people under the age of about thirty will often mutually adopt
       <i>
        te
       </i>
       automatically in informal situations. A notable example is the Internet: strangers meeting online use the informal forms of address virtually exclusively, regardless of age or status differences.
      </p>
      <p>
       Nevertheless, formal forms of address are alive and well in Hungarian:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The third-person verb conjugation is the primary basis of formal address. The choice of which
        <i>
         pronoun
        </i>
        to use, however, is fraught with difficulty (and indeed a common solution when in doubt is to simply avoid using any pronoun at all, using the addressee's name or title instead).
        <ul>
         <li>
          The pronoun
          <i>
           maga
          </i>
          (plural
          <i>
           maguk
          </i>
          ), for instance, is considered the basic formal equivalent of "you", but may not be used indiscriminately, as it tends to imply an existing or desired personal acquaintance. (It would not, for instance, ordinarily be used in a conversation where the relative social roles are predominantly important – say, between professor and student.) Typical situations where
          <i>
           maga
          </i>
          might be used are, e.g., distant relatives, neighbours, fellow travellers on the train, or at the hairdresser's. If one already knows these people, they may even take offence if one were to address them more formally. On the other hand, some urbanites tend to avoid
          <i>
           maga
          </i>
          , finding it too rural, old-fashioned, offensive or even intimate. – Note that
          <i>
           maga
          </i>
          coincides with the
          <a href="/wiki/Reflexive_pronoun" title="Reflexive pronoun">
           reflexive pronoun
          </a>
          (cf. him/herself), so e.g. the sentence
          <i>
           Megütötte magát?
          </i>
          can have three meanings: "Did he hit himself?", "Did he hit you?" or "Did you hit yourself?".
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           Ön
          </i>
          (plural
          <i>
           önök
          </i>
          ) is the formal, official and impersonal "you". It is the form used when people take part in a situation merely as representatives of
          <a href="/wiki/Role" title="Role">
           social roles
          </a>
          , where personal acquaintance is not a factor. It is thus used in institutions, business, bureaucracy, advertisements, by broadcasters, by shopkeepers to their customers, and whenever one wishes to maintain one's distance. It is less typical of rural areas or small towns, more typical of cities. It's often capitalized in letters.
         </li>
         <li>
          Other pronouns are nowadays rare, restricted to rural, jocular, dialect, or old-fashioned speech. Such are, for instance,
          <i>
           kend
          </i>
          and
          <i>
           kegyed
          </i>
          .
         </li>
         <li>
          There is a wide spectrum of third-person address that avoids the above pronouns entirely; preferring to substitute various combinations of the addressee's names and/or titles. Thus, for instance, a university student might ask
          <i>
           mit gondol X. tanár úr?
          </i>
          ("What does Professor X. think?", meant for the addressee) rather than using the insufficiently formal
          <i>
           maga
          </i>
          or the overly impersonal
          <i>
           ön
          </i>
          . If the difference in rank is not to be emphasized, it is perfectly acceptable to use the addressed person's first name instead of a second-person pronoun, e.g.
          <i>
           Megkérném arra Pétert, hogy…
          </i>
          ("I'd like to ask [you,] Peter to…"). (Note that these are possible because the formal second-person conjugation of verbs is the same as the third-person conjugation.)
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        Finally, the
        <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">
         auxiliary verb
        </a>
        <i>
         tetszik
        </i>
        (lit. "it pleases [you]") is an indirect alternative (or, perhaps, supplement) to direct address with the third or even second person. In terms of grammar, it can only be applied if the addressed person is mentioned in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nominative" title="Nominative">
         nominative
        </a>
        , otherwise it is replaced by forms with the name or
        <i>
         maga.
        </i>
        It is very polite (sometimes seen as over-polite) and not as formal as the
        <i>
         Ön
        </i>
        form. Children usually address adults outside their family this way. Adults may address more distant relatives,
        <a href="/wiki/Housekeeper_(domestic_worker)" title="Housekeeper (domestic worker)">
         housekeepers
        </a>
        and older persons using this form, and some men habitually address older or younger women this way (this is slightly old-fashioned).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <br/>
       It is important to keep in mind that formal conjugation doesn't automatically imply politeness or vice versa; these factors are independent of each other. For example,
       <i>
        Mit parancsolsz?
       </i>
       "What would you like to have?" (literally, "What do you command?") is in the informal conjugation, while it can be extremely polite, making it possible to express one's honour towards people one has previously established a friendly relationship with. On the other hand,
       <i>
        Mit akar?
       </i>
       "What do you want?" is expressed with the formal conjugation, nevertheless it may sound rude and aggressive; the formal conjugation does not soften this tone in any way.
      </p>
      <table align="center" class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th colspan="2">

         <br/>

        </th>
        <th colspan="3">
         Example: "you" in the accusative
         <br/>
         "I saw you yesterday on the television."
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>

        </th>
        <td colspan="2">

         </i>

         <i>
          Látta
          <b>

          tegnap a tévében.

          </b>
         </i>
        </td>

          </b>

         <br/>

          </b>
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

        </th>

          <b>

          </b>

          <b>

          </b>

         </i>
        </td>
        <td colspan="3">
         &lt;The name or
         <i>

         </i>
         is used instead&gt;
         <br/>
         <i>
