          <b>
           Mari nénit
          </b>
          ** a tévében.
         </i>
         <br/>
         OR
         <i>
          Láttam tegnap
          <b>
           magát
          </b>
          a tévében.
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        *
        <i>
         tanár úr
        </i>
        is a form of addressing for professors (cf. "Sir");
        <i>
         tanár urat
        </i>
        is the accusative. Other forms of addressing are also possible, to avoid specifying the
        <i>
         maga
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         ön
        </i>
        pronouns.
       </dd>
       <dd>
        **
        <i>
         Mari nénit
        </i>
        is an example name in the accusative (cf. "Aunt Mary").
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Turkic">
        Turkic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Turkic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Turkish">
        Turkish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Turkish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In modern
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">
        Turkish
       </a>
       , the T–V distinction is strong. Family members and friends speak to one another using the second-person singular
       <i>
        sen
       </i>
       , and adults use
       <i>
        sen
       </i>
       to address minors. In formal situations (business, customer-clerk, and colleague relationships, or meeting people for the first time) the plural second-person
       <i>
        siz
       </i>
       is used almost exclusively. In very formal situations, the double plural second-person
       <i>
        sizler
       </i>
       may be used to address a much-respected person. Rarely, the third-person plural form of the verb (but not the pronoun) may be used to emphasize utmost respect. In the imperative, there are three forms: second person singular for informal, second person plural for formal, and second person double plural for very formal situations:
       <i>
        gel
       </i>
       (second person singular, informal),
       <i>
        gelin
       </i>
       (second person plural, formal), and
       <i>
        geliniz
       </i>
       (double second person plural, very formal). The very formal forms are not frequently used in spoken Turkish, but is pretty common in written directives, such as manuals and warning signs.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Uyghur">
        Uyghur
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Uyghur">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_language" title="Uyghur language">
        Uyghur
       </a>
       is notable for using four different forms, to distinguish both singular and plural in both formal and informal registers. The informal plural
       <i>
        silär
       </i>
       originated as a contraction of
       <i>
        sizlär
       </i>
       , which uses a regular plural ending. In Old Turkic, as still in modern Turkish,
       <i>
        siz
       </i>
       was the original second-person plural. However, in modern Uyghur
       <i>
        siz
       </i>
       has become restricted to the formal singular, requiring the plural suffix -
       <i>
        lär
       </i>
       for the plurals.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Siz
       </i>
       as the formal singular pronoun is characteristic of the Ürümchi dialect, which is the Uyghur literary standard. In Turfan they say
       <i>
        sili
       </i>
       and in Kashgar dialect,
       <i>
        özlär
       </i>
       .
       <i>
        Sili
       </i>
       is also used in other areas sometimes, while in literary Uyghur
       <i>
        özlär
       </i>
       as a singular pronoun is considered a "hyperdeferential" level of respect; the deferential plural form is
       <i>
        härqaysiliri
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Northwest_Caucasian">
        Northwest Caucasian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Northwest Caucasian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ubykh">
        Ubykh
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: Ubykh">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In the extinct
       <a href="/wiki/Ubykh_language" title="Ubykh language">
        Ubykh language
       </a>
       , the T–V distinction was most notable between a man and his mother-in-law, where the plural form
       <i>
        sʸæghʷa
       </i>
       supplanted the singular
       <i>
        wæghʷa
       </i>
       very frequently, possibly under the influence of
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">
        Turkish
       </a>
       . The distinction was upheld less frequently in other relationships, but did still occur.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Semitic">
        Semitic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: Semitic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Arabic">
        Arabic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=71" title="Edit section: Arabic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">
        Modern Standard Arabic
       </a>
       uses the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majestic_plural" title="Majestic plural">
        majestic plural
       </a>
       form of the
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
        second person
       </a>
       (أنتم
       <i>
        antum
       </i>
       ) in respectful address.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       It is restricted to highly formal contexts, generally relating to politics and government. However, several
       <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">
        varieties of Arabic
       </a>
       have a clearer T–V distinction. The most developed is in
       <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">
        Egyptian Arabic
       </a>
       , which uses حضرتك
       <i>
        ḥaḍritak
       </i>
       (literally, "Your
       <a href="/wiki/Grace_(style)" title="Grace (style)">
        Grace
       </a>
       "), سعادتك
       <i>
        sa`adtak
       </i>
       and سيادتك
       <i>
        siyadtak
       </i>
       (literally, "Your Lordship") as the "V" terms, depending on context, while أنت
       <i>
        inta
       </i>
       is the "T" term.
       <i>
        Ḥaḍritak
       </i>
       is the most usual "V" term, with
       <i>
        sa`adtak
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        siyadtak
       </i>
       being reserved for situations where the addressee is of very high social standing (e.g. a high-ranking government official or a powerful businessman). Finally, the "V" term is used only with social superiors (including elders); unfamiliar people perceived to be of similar or lower social standing to the speaker are addressed with the
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       term
       <i>
        inta
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hebrew">
        Hebrew
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=72" title="Edit section: Hebrew">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In modern
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       , there is a T–V distinction used in a set of very formal occasions, for example, a lawyer addressing a judge, or when speaking to rabbis. The second person singular אַתָּה (
       <i>
        ʔaˈta
       </i>
       , masculine) or אַתְּ (
       <i>
        ʔat
       </i>
       , feminine) are the usual form of address in all other situations, e.g. when addressing ministers or members of the
       <a href="/wiki/Knesset" title="Knesset">
        Knesset
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The formal form of address when speaking to a person of higher authority is the third person singular using the person's title without the use of the pronoun. Thus, a rabbi could be asked:
       <span class="nowrap">
        ?כְּבוֹד הָרַב יִרְצֶה לֶאֱכֹל
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        kəˈvod haˈʁav yiʁˈtse leʔeˈχol
       </i>
       , "would the honorable rabbi like to eat?") or a judge told: כְּבוֹדוֹ דָּן בְּבַקָּשָׁתִי (
       <i>
        kəvoˈdo dan bəvakaʃaˈti
       </i>
       , "his honour is considering my request").
      </p>
      <p>
       Other persons of authority are normally addressed by their title only, rather than by name, using the second person singular. For example, officers and commanders in the army are addressed as הַמְּפַקֵּד (
       <i>
        haməfaˈked
       </i>
       , "the commander") by troops.
      </p>
      <p>
       In non-Hebrew-speaking Jewish culture, the second-person form of address is similarly avoided in cases of higher authority (e.g., a student in a
       <a href="/wiki/Yeshiva" title="Yeshiva">
        yeshiva
       </a>
       would be far more likely to say in a classroom discussion "yesterday the rabbi told us..." than "yesterday you told us..."). However, this usage is limited to more conservative (i.e. Orthodox) circles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dravidian">
        Dravidian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=73" title="Edit section: Dravidian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tamil">
        Tamil
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=74" title="Edit section: Tamil">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">
        Tamil
       </a>
       , the second-person singular pronoun நீ
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [niː]
       </span>
       and its derived forms are used to address children, (younger or very close) members of the family and to people who are younger than the speaker. The second person plural pronoun நீங்கள்
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [niːŋgʌɭ]
       </span>
       is used to address elders (also within the extended family), teachers, people who are older than the speaker and anyone whom the speaker does not personally know, especially in formal situations.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sino-Tibetan">
        Sino-Tibetan
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=75" title="Edit section: Sino-Tibetan">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese">
        Chinese
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=76" title="Edit section: Chinese">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <span id="Mandarin_Chinese">
       </span>
       <span id="Mandarin">
       </span>
       <span id="Shanghainese">
       </span>
       <span id="Cantonese">
       </span>
       <span id="Minnan">
       </span>
      </p>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_honorifics" title="Chinese honorifics">
        Chinese honorifics
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_naming_taboo" title="Chinese naming taboo">
        Chinese naming taboo
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">
        Chinese culture
       </a>
       has taken
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_names" title="Chinese names">
        naming
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_honorifics" title="Chinese honorifics">
        forms of address
       </a>
       very seriously, strictly regulating which people were permitted to use which terms in conversation or in writing. The extreme example is the 1777 execution of
       <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xihou" title="Wang Xihou">
        Wang Xihou
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nine_exterminations" title="Nine exterminations">
        his entire family
       </a>
       and the confiscation of their entire estate as his penalty for writing the
       <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">
        Qianlong Emperor
       </a>
       's personal name as part of a criticism of the
       <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Dictionary" title="Kangxi Dictionary">
        Kangxi Dictionary
       </a>
       . Many honorifics and niceties of address fell by the wayside during the
       <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">
        Cultural Revolution
       </a>
       of the late 1960s amid
       <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">
        Mao Zedong
       </a>
       's campaign against the "
       <a href="/wiki/Four_Olds" title="Four Olds">
        Four Olds
       </a>
       ". This included an attempt to eradicate expressions of deference to teachers and to others seen as preserving "counter-revolutionary" modes of thought. The defeat of the Maoist
       <a href="/wiki/Gang_of_Four" title="Gang of Four">
        Gang of Four
       </a>
       in the late 1970s and continuing
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_economic_reform" title="Chinese economic reform">
        reforms
       </a>
       since the 1980s has, however, permitted a return of such traditional and regional expressions.
      </p>
      <p>
       Historically, the T–V distinction was observed among the Chinese by avoiding
       <i>
        any
       </i>
       use of common pronouns in addressing a respected audience. Instead, third-person
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_honorifics#Addressing_or_referring_to_others" title="Chinese honorifics">
        honorifics
       </a>
       and respectful titles were employed. One aspect of such respectful address was avoiding the use of the
       <i>
        first
       </i>
       -person pronoun as well, instead choosing a (typically humble)
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_honorifics#Referring_to_oneself" title="Chinese honorifics">
        epithet
       </a>
       in its place. The extreme of this practice occurred when
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi_Huangdi" title="Shi Huangdi">
        Shi Huangdi
       </a>
       abrogated the then-current first-person pronoun
       <span lang="zh">
        朕
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        zhèn
       </i>
       ); the present first-person pronoun
       <span lang="zh">
        我
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        wǒ
       </i>
       ) subsequently developed out of the habit of referring to "this [worthless] body", the character's original meaning.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An important difference between the T–V distinction in Chinese compared with modern European languages is that Chinese culture considers the relative age of the speakers an important aspect of their
       <a href="/wiki/Social_distance" title="Social distance">
        social distance
       </a>
       . This is especially strong within families: while the speakers of European languages may generally prefer forms of address such as "father" or "grandpa", Chinese speakers consider using the personal names of elders such a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_naming_taboo" title="Chinese naming taboo">
        taboo
       </a>
       that they may not even
       <i>
        know
       </i>
       the given names of grandparents who live in the same apartment. While strictures against writing the personal name of any ancestor of the last seven generations are no longer observed, it remains very uncommon to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_names" title="Chinese names">
        name children
       </a>
       for any living relative: younger people using the name freely would disrespect the original bearer.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the present day, the informal second-person pronoun is
       <span lang="zh">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BD%A0" title="wikt:你">
         你
        </a>
       </span>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">
        Mandarin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        nǐ
       </i>
       ;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minnan_language" title="Minnan language">
        Minnan
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        lí
       </i>
       ) and the honorific pronoun is
       <span lang="zh">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%82%A8" title="wikt:您">
         您
        </a>
       </span>
       (Mandarin:
       <i>
        nín
       </i>
       ; Minnan:
       <i>
        lín
       </i>
       ). Much like European languages, the honorific form developed out of an earlier second-person plural: during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jurchen_Dynasty" title="Jurchen Dynasty">
        Jin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">
        Yuan
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_dynasties" title="List of Chinese dynasties">
        dynasties
       </a>
       , the Mandarin dialects mutated
       <span lang="zh">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BD%A0%E6%AF%8F" title="wikt:你每">
         你每
        </a>
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        nǐměi
       </i>
       ) into
       <span lang="zh">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BD%A0%E5%80%91" title="wikt:你們">
         你們
        </a>
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        nǐmen
       </i>
       ) and then into
       <span lang="zh">
        您
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (A similar form –
       <span lang="zh">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%80%B9" title="wikt:怹">
         怹
        </a>
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        tān
       </i>
       – developed for the third-person singular but is now generally unused.) It is worth noting that the T–V distinction in Mandarin does
       <i>
        not
       </i>
       connote a distance or lack of intimacy between the speakers (as implied, e.g., in the French
       <i>
        vous
       </i>
       ). On the contrary, it is often noted that the respectful form contains the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_radical" title="Chinese radical">
        radical
       </a>
       for "heart" (
       <span lang="zh">
        心
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        xīn
       </i>
       ); although this is actually for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character#Rebus" title="Chinese character">
        phonetic reasons
       </a>
       , the implication is that the addressee is loved and cherished by the speaker.
      </p>
      <p>
       Most
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_southern_China" title="Northern and southern China">
        southern dialects
       </a>
       , however, do not make this distinction in speech at all.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cantonese_language" title="Cantonese language">
        Cantonese
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shanghainese_language" title="Shanghainese language">
        Shanghainese
       </a>
       speakers learn to
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Written_Chinese" title="Written Chinese">
         write
        </a>
       </i>
       both forms in school but pronounce them identically: the Cantonese as
       <i>
        nei
       </i>
       <sup>
        5
       </sup>
       and the Shanghainese as
       <i>
        nóng
       </i>
       . Formality is still respected, but their languages – like
       <a href="#Japanese">
        Japanese
       </a>
       and
       <a href="#Vietnamese">
        Vietnamese
       </a>
       – retain the earlier Chinese tradition of employing epithets or honorifics instead of using any pronouns at all when showing formal respect.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Japonic">
        Japonic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=77" title="Edit section: Japonic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese">
        Japanese
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=78" title="Edit section: Japanese">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_pronouns" title="Japanese pronouns">
        Japanese pronouns
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_honorifics" title="Japanese honorifics">
        Japanese honorifics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Honorific_speech_in_Japanese" title="Honorific speech in Japanese">
        Honorific speech in Japanese
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Under heavy
       <a href="#Chinese">
        Chinese
       </a>
       influence, traditional
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_culture" title="Japanese culture">
        Japanese culture
       </a>
       eschewed the use of common pronouns in formal speech; similarly, the Chinese first-person singular
       <span lang="ja">
        朕
       </span>
       (
       <span lang="ja">
        ちん
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        chin
       </i>
       ) was arrogated to the personal use of the
       <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">
        emperor
       </a>
       . The formality of Japanese culture was such that its
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_pronouns#Archaic_personal_pronouns" title="Japanese pronouns">
        original pronouns
       </a>
       have largely ceased to be used at all. Some linguists therefore argue that
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">
        Japanese
       </a>
       lacks
       <i>
        any
       </i>
       pronouns whatsoever, but – although it is a larger and more complex group of words than most languages employ –
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_pronouns" title="Japanese pronouns">
        Japanese pronouns
       </a>
       do exist, having developed out of the most common epithets used to express different relationships and relative degrees of social status. As in
       <a href="#Korean">
        Korean
       </a>
       , polite language encompasses not only these specific pronouns but also suffixes and vocabulary as well.
      </p>
      <p>
       Most commonly,
       <span lang="ja">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%90%9B" title="wikt:君">
         君
        </a>
       </span>
       (
       <span lang="ja">
        きみ
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        kimi
       </i>
       ,
       <small>
        orig.
       </small>
       "prince", "lord") is used informally as the second-person singular and
       <span lang="ja">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%B2%B4%E6%96%B9" title="wikt:貴方">
         貴方
        </a>
       </span>
       (
       <span lang="ja">
        あなた
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        anata
       </i>
       ,
       <small>
        lit.
       </small>
       "dear one") is the most common polite equivalent, but is also commonly used by women towards an intimate as a term of endearment.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The pronoun
       <span lang="ja">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%B2%B4%E6%A7%98" title="wikt:貴様">
         貴様
        </a>
       </span>
       (
       <span lang="ja">
        きさま
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        kisama
       </i>
       ) is illustrative of the complexity that can be involved, though, in that its literal meaning is quite flattering –
       <small>
        lit.
       </small>
       "dear and honorable sir" – but its ironic use has made it a strong insult in modern Japanese. Similarly,
       <span lang="ja">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%BE%A1%E5%89%8D" title="wikt:御前">
         御前
        </a>
       </span>
       (
       <span lang="ja">
        おまえ
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        omae
       </i>
       ) –
       <small>
        lit.
       </small>
       "(one who is) before (me)" – was traditionally a respectful pronoun used toward aristocrats and religious figureheads, but today is considered very informal and impolite, yet also commonly used by husbands towards their wives in an endearing manner.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Austro-Asiatic">
        Austro-Asiatic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=79" title="Edit section: Austro-Asiatic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnamese">
        Vietnamese
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=80" title="Edit section: Vietnamese">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_pronouns" title="Vietnamese pronouns">
        Vietnamese pronouns
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Under heavy
       <a href="#Chinese">
        Chinese
       </a>
       influence,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vietnamese_culture" title="Vietnamese culture">
        Vietnamese culture
       </a>
       has eschewed the use of
       <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_pronouns#true_pronouns" title="Vietnamese pronouns">
        common pronouns
       </a>
       in formal speech; similarly, the Chinese first-person singular
       <span lang="zh">
        朕
       </span>
       (Vietnamese:
       <i>
        trẫm
       </i>
       ) was arrogated to the personal use of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Vietnam" title="Emperor of Vietnam">
        emperor
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In modern Vietnamese, only the first-person singular
       <i>
        tôi
       </i>
       is in common use as a respectful pronoun; any other pronoun should be replaced with the subject's name or with an appropriate epithet, title, or relationship in polite formal speech. Similar to modern Chinese (but to a much greater extent), modern Vietnamese also frequently replaces
       <i>
        informal
       </i>
       pronouns with
       <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_pronouns#Kinship_terms" title="Vietnamese pronouns">
        kinship terms
       </a>
       in many situations. The somewhat insulting second-person singular
       <i>
        mày
       </i>
       is also frequently used in informal situations among young Vietnamese.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Tai.E2.80.93Kadai_languages">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tai–Kadai_languages">
        Tai–Kadai languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=81" title="Edit section: Tai–Kadai languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Thai">
        Thai
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=82" title="Edit section: Thai">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">
        Thai
       </a>
       , first, second, and third person pronouns vary in formality according to the social standing of the speaker and the referent and the relationship between them. For a non-exhaustive list of Thai second person pronouns, see
       <a class="external free" href="http://www.into-asia.com/thai_language/grammar/you.php" rel="nofollow">
        http://www.into-asia.com/thai_language/grammar/you.php
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Austronesian">
        Austronesian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=83" title="Edit section: Austronesian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesian">
        Indonesian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=84" title="Edit section: Indonesian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">
        Indonesian
       </a>
       , the T–V distinction is extremely important; addressing a stranger with the pronoun
       <i>
        kau
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        kamu
       </i>
       (you) is considered rude and impolite (unless the stranger is, for example, a child). When addressing a stranger or someone older, typically
       <i>
        Bu
       </i>
       ('ma'am') or
       <i>
        Pak
       </i>
       ('sir') is used. People also use
       <i>
        mas
       </i>
       (Javanese for 'older brother') or
       <i>
        mbak
       </i>
       (Javanese for 'older sister') when addressing someone that is not old enough to be called
       <i>
        Bu
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Pak
       </i>
       . There are variations in different areas. If the situation is more formal, such as in meetings or news broadcasting,
       <i>
        Anda
       </i>
       is always used, even if those addressed would otherwise be addressed by
       <i>
        kau
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        kamu
       </i>
       in informal situations. A more informal pronoun, written
       <i>
        lu
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        lo
       </i>
       , or sometimes as
       <i>
        loe
       </i>
       (originated from Hokkien language) is considered very impolite. This is normally used by teenagers (particularly in urban centers) to their peers. Adults can sometimes be heard using this pronoun with their close friends or when they are angry.
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        <i>
         Lu siap?
        </i>
        ('Are you ready?'): This form is used between friends in very informal situations without the presence of someone who has higher status.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Kamu siap?
        </i>
        ('Are you ready?'): This form is used between friends in either informal or formal situations without the presence of someone who has higher status.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Anda siap?
        </i>
        ('Are you ready?'): This form is used between friends in formal situations, between business partners, or with someone who has higher status.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Apakah Anda siap?
        </i>
        (Are you ready?): This form is used between friends in very formal situations, among strangers, or toward someone who has higher status. Note that
        <i>
         Apakah
        </i>
        is an optional question word that is used in close-ended questions (similar to the use of 'to be' and other auxiliary verbs to form close-ended questions in English). This is a form of
        <i>
         Bahasa Baku
        </i>
        .
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       Similarly,
       <i>
        kalian
       </i>
       dan
       <i>
        Anda
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        Anda sekalian
       </i>
       are used.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tagalog">
        Tagalog
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=85" title="Edit section: Tagalog">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">
        Tagalog
       </a>
       , the familiar second person is
       <i>
        ikáw/ka
       </i>
       (in the nominative case). This is replaced by
       <i>
        kayó
       </i>
       (which is actually the second person plural) when the situation calls for a more polite tone. The pronoun
       <i>
        kayó
       </i>
       is accompanied by the particle
       <i>
        pô
       </i>
       . This form is generally used to show respect to close, older relatives. This is also the form expected when talking with friends of parents or grandparents.
      </p>
      <p>
       However, when formality is required, the third person plural (
       <i>
        silá
       </i>
       ) is used instead. This form is used when talking with complete strangers or people with high ranks, such as government officials.
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        Sino ka? (Who are you?) [Used to ask for the identity of a person of equal rank, such as a student to a fellow student. However, this question sounds impolite.]
       </li>
       <li>
        Sino pô kayó? (Who are you?) [This form implies that the speaker believes the person addressed is related to them or a relative, and just wants to confirm the relationship.]
       </li>
       <li>
        Sino pô silá? (Who are you, Sir/Ma'am?) [Though '
        <b>
         pô'
        </b>
        does not really translate as '
        <b>
         Sir'
        </b>
        or '
        <b>
         Ma'am'
        </b>
        , the question gives us an idea that the person addressed is a complete stranger and the speaker has no idea who they are.]
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       Younger generations usually mix these forms of address, thus asking someone
       <i>
        Sino ka pô ba?
       </i>
       in an attempt to sound polite towards a total stranger. This and other nonstandard variants are very widespread especially in Metro Manila and surrounding suburbs.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_languages">
        Other languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=86" title="Edit section: Other languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Basque">
        Basque
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=87" title="Edit section: Basque">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Basque_grammar#Personal_pronouns" title="Basque grammar">
        Basque grammar: Personal pronouns
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">
        Basque
       </a>
       has two levels of formality in every dialect, which are
       <b>
        hi
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        zu
       </b>
       ; Nevertheless, in some areas of Gipuzkoa and Biscay, the respectful form
       <b>
        berori
       </b>
       is still used by some speakers, just as the familiar
       <b>
        xu
       </b>
       in some areas of the Eastern Low Navarrese dialect, when addressing children and close friends. Most speakers only use the
       <i>
        zu
       </i>
       form (
       <i>
        zuka
       </i>
       level) and that is the usual one used in methods, slogans... although the
       <i>
        hi
       </i>
       form (
       <i>
        hika
       </i>
       ) is very common in villages.
      </p>
      <p>
       The neutral or formal one is
       <i>
        zu
       </i>
       , which originally used to be the plural form of the second person. The informal one is
       <i>
        hi
       </i>
       , whose use is limited to some specific situations: among close friends, to children (children never use it when addressing their parents, neither the spouses among them), when talking to a younger person, to animals (cattle, pets...), in monologues, and when speaking angrily to somebody. Their common plural form is
       <i>
        zuek
       </i>
       , whenever the speaker is talking to a group of listeners who would all be individually addressed with the form
       <i>
        zu
       </i>
       , or the form
       <i>
        hi
       </i>
       , or both (a conversation where some listeners are addressed as
       <i>
        zu
       </i>
       —i.e., somebody's parents, for instance— and others as
       <i>
        hi
       </i>
       —the speaker's siblings—).
      </p>
      <p>
       Unlike
       <i>
        zu
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        hi
       </i>
       often makes a distinction whether the addressed one is a male or a female. For example:
       <i>
        duk
       </i>
       (thou, male, hast) and
       <i>
        dun
       </i>
       (thou, female, hast). The use of the
       <b>
        hika
       </b>
       level requires the allocutive agreement (
       <i>
        hitano
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        zeharkako hika
       </i>
       , i.e., indirect
       <i>
        hika
       </i>
       ) in non-subordinate sentences to mark this distinction for the first and third person verbs. Those allocutive forms are found in the Indicative and Conditional moods, but never in the Subjunctive and Imperative moods, with the one exception of
       <i>
        goazemak
       </i>
       (let's go, said to a male) and
       <i>
        goazeman
       </i>
       (said to a female) in Western dialects, opposed to
       <i>
        goazen
       </i>
       , the neutral form. For example:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         du
        </i>
        (neutral, s/he has, neutral form),
        <i>
         dik
        </i>
        (s/he has, male thou) and
        <i>
         din
        </i>
        (s/he has, female thou), as in
        <i>
         aitak ikasi du
        </i>
        (polite: Dad has learned it),
        <i>
         aitak ikasi dik
        </i>
        (informal, said to a male), and
        <i>
         aitak ikasi din
        </i>
        (informal, said to a female).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         dio
        </i>
        (neutral, s/he has it for him / her),
        <i>
         ziok
        </i>
        (familiar, s/he has it for him / her, said to a male), and
        <i>
         zion
        </i>
        (familiar, s/he has it for him / her, said to a female), as in
        <i>
         aitak erosi dio
        </i>
        (polite: Dad has bought it for him / her),
        <i>
         aitak erosi ziok
        </i>
        (informal, said to a male), and
        <i>
         aitak erosi zion
        </i>
        (informal, said to a female).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         nintzen
        </i>
        (neutral, I was),
        <i>
         ninduan
        </i>
        (familiar, said to a male), and
        <i>
         nindunan
        </i>
        (familiar, said to a female), as in
        <i>
         hona etorri nintzen
        </i>
        (polite: I came here),
        <i>
         hona etorri ninduan
        </i>
        (informal, said to a male), and
        <i>
         hona etorri nindunan
        </i>
        (informal, said to a female).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Nevertheless, if any of the allocutive sentences becomes subordinate, the formal one is used:
       <i>
        aitak ikasi duelako
       </i>
       (because Dad has learned it),
       <i>
        aitak erosi diolako
       </i>
       (because Dad bought it for him / her), and
       <i>
        hona etorri nintzenean
       </i>
       (when I came here).
      </p>
      <p>
       On the other hand, in past tense verbal forms, no distinction is made when is the addressee is the subject or the direct object in the sentence. For example:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         hintzen
        </i>
        , in
        <i>
         etxera joan hintzen
        </i>
        (thou wentst home),
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         huen
        </i>
        , in
        <i>
         filma ikusi huen
        </i>
        (thou sawst the film),
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         hindugun
        </i>
        , in
        <i>
         ikusi hindugun
        </i>
        (we saw thee).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       But if the familiar second-person appears in the verb, or if the verb is an allocutive form in a non-dependent clause, the masculine and feminine forms differ. For example:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         genian / geninan
        </i>
        (we had something for thee, male / female):
        <i>
         hiri eman genian, Piarres
        </i>
        (we gave it to thee, Peter), and
        <i>
         hiri eman geninan, Maddi
        </i>
        (we gave it to thee, Mary).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         geniean / genienan
        </i>
        (male allocutive / female allocutive, we had something for them):
        <i>
         haiei eman geniean, Piarres
        </i>
        (we gave it to them, Peter), and
        <i>
         haiei eman genienan, Maddi
        </i>
        (we gave it to them, Mary). Their corresponding neutral form is
        <i>
         haiei eman genien
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         banekian erantzuna
        </i>
        (I knew the answer, said to a male), and
        <i>
         banekinan erantzuna
        </i>
        (I knew the answer, said to a female). Their corresponding neutral form is
        <i>
         banekien erantzuna
        </i>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The friendly
       <i>
        xu
       </i>
       form or
       <b>
        xuketa
       </b>
       resembles the
       <i>
        zuka
       </i>
       forms of the verbs, and includes another kind of allocutive, as
       <i>
        hika
       </i>
       : cf.
       <i>
        egia erran dut
       </i>
       (formal: I told the truth),
       <i>
        egia erran diat
       </i>
       (informal, said to a male),
       <i>
        egia erran dinat
       </i>
       (informal, said to a female),
       <i>
        egia erran dautzut
       </i>
       (in formal Eastern Low Navarrese, I told
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       the truth) and
       <i>
        egia erran dixut
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        xuketa
       </i>
       ). It is mainly used among relatives and close friends.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        berori
       </i>
       form or
       <b>
        berorika
       </b>
       is very formal, and hardly used nowadays, mainly in some areas of Biscay and Gipuzkoa, to address priests, the elderly, judges and the nobility. Verbs are inflected in their singular third form, like in Italian (
       <i>
        (Lei) è molto gentile
       </i>
       , opposed to
       <i>
        (tu) sei molto gentile
       </i>
       , you are very nice / thou art very nice) or the Spanish
       <i>
        (usted) es muy amable
       </i>
       , opposed to
       <i>
        (tú) eres muy amable
       </i>
       ):
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        neutral:
        <i>
         zuk badakizu hori
        </i>
        (you know it, formal), and
        <i>
         zu, eser zaitez hemen
        </i>
        (you, sit here),
       </li>
       <li>
        familiar:
        <i>
         hik badakik hori
        </i>
        (thou knowest that, said to a male),
        <i>
         hik badakin hori
        </i>
        (to a female), and
        <i>
         hi, eser hadi hemen
        </i>
        (sit here, for both genres),
       </li>
       <li>
        very formal:
        <i>
         berorrek badaki hori
        </i>
        (you know that: cf.
        <i>
         hark badaki hori
        </i>
        , s/he knows that, neutral), and
        <i>
         berori, eser bedi hemen
        </i>
        (you, sit down here: cf
        <i>
         hura, eser bedi hemen
        </i>
        , let him sit down here).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Unlike the
       <i>
        hika
       </i>
       level,
       <i>
        berorika
       </i>
       has no allocutive forms.
      </p>
      <p>
       The extinct dialect of Erronkari or Roncal, spoken in the easternmost area of Navarre, presented a four-levelled system:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        neutral or
        <i>
         zuketza
        </i>
        , the local equivalent of
        <i>
         zuka
        </i>
        :
        <i>
         etxeara xuan zra
        </i>
        (you went home, you have gone home),
        <i>
         etxeara xuan naz
        </i>
        (I went home, I have gone home),
       </li>
       <li>
        informal or
        <i>
         yiketza
        </i>
        , which corresponds to
        <i>
         hika
        </i>
        :
        <i>
         etxeara xuan yaz
        </i>
        , (thou wentst home, thou hast gone home),
        <i>
         etxeara xuan nuk
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         etxeara xuan nun
        </i>
        (I went home, I have been home, said to a male / to a female),
       </li>
       <li>
        familiar or
        <i>
         tzuketza
        </i>
        , like the Eastern Navarrese
        <i>
         xuka
        </i>
        :
        <i>
         etxeara xuan nuzu
        </i>
        (I went home, I have been home),
       </li>
       <li>
        and
        <i>
         orika
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         duka
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         duketza
        </i>
        , the local form of
        <i>
         berorika
        </i>
        :
        <i>
         ori etxeara xin da
        </i>
        (you went home, you have been home).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Constructed_languages">
        Constructed languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=88" title="Edit section: Constructed languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Esperanto">
        Esperanto
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=89" title="Edit section: Esperanto">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Esperanto" title="Esperanto">
        Esperanto
       </a>
       is a T–V-distinguishing language, but usually
       <i>
        vi
       </i>
       is used for both singular and plural, just like
       <i>
        you
       </i>
       in modern English. An informal second person singular pronoun,
       <i>
        ci
       </i>
       , does exist, but it is rarely used in practice. It is mainly intended to make the familiar/respectful distinction when translating (literature, for example) from languages that do have the T–V distinction.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some have imagined
       <i>
        ci
       </i>
       as an archaic term that was used before and then fell out of common usage; however, this is not true. It has appeared only sometimes in experimental language. In standard Esperanto,
       <i>
        vi
       </i>
       has always been used since the beginning. For example,
       <i>
        ci
       </i>
       appears in neither the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fundamento_de_Esperanto" title="Fundamento de Esperanto">
         Fundamenta Gramatiko
        </a>
       </i>
       nor the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Unua_Libro" title="Unua Libro">
         Unua Libro
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       But, especially in some circles, people have started to use
       <i>
        ci
       </i>
       in practical language, mainly as the familiar and intimate singular, reserving
       <i>
        vi
       </i>
       for the plural and formal singular. Others use
       <i>
        ci
       </i>
       as singular and
       <i>
        vi
       </i>
       as plural regardless of formality.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ido">
        Ido
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=90" title="Edit section: Ido">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ido_(language)" title="Ido (language)">
        Ido
       </a>
       , in theory
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is limited to friends and family, whereas
       <i>
        vu
       </i>
       is used anywhere else. However, many users actually adapt the practice in their own mother tongue and use
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        vu
       </i>
       accordingly. In the plural, though, the only form in use is
       <i>
        vi
       </i>
       , which does not distinguish between formal and informal address.
      </p>
      <p>
       In all cases, an -n is added to the original pronoun to indicate a direct object that precedes its own verb:
       <i>
        Me amoras tu
       </i>
       (I love you) becomes
       <i>
        Tun me amoras
       </i>
       if the direct object takes the first place, for example for emphatic purposes.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Tolkien.27s_High_Elvish">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tolkien's_High_Elvish">
        Tolkien's High Elvish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=91" title="Edit section: Tolkien's High Elvish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Quenya" title="Quenya">
        High Elvish
       </a>
       , self-named
       <b>
        Quenya
       </b>
       , there is a distinction between singular informal
       <i>
        tyë
       </i>
       and singular formal
       <i>
        lyë
       </i>
       . The plural of both forms is
       <i>
        lë
       </i>
       . The formal form is expected between all but family members and close friends. The appendices to
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Rings" title="Lord of the Rings">
         Lord of the Rings
        </a>
       </i>
       state that
       <a href="/wiki/Westron" title="Westron">
        Westron
       </a>
       followed a similar pattern, although the dialect of Shire had largely lost the formal form.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Related_verbs.2C_nouns_and_pronouns">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Related_verbs,_nouns_and_pronouns">
        Related verbs, nouns and pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=T%E2%80%93V_distinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=92" title="Edit section: Related verbs, nouns and pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Some languages have a verb to describe the fact of using either a
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       or a
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       form. Some also have a related noun or pronoun. The English words are used to refer only to English usage in the past, not to usage in other languages. The analogous distinction may be expressed as "to use first names" or "to be on familiar terms (with someone)".
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
         T verb
        </th>
        <th>
         V verb
        </th>
        <th>
         T noun
        </th>
        <th>
         V noun
        </th>
       </tr>

        <li id="cite_note-31">

           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <i>
           Þū
          </i>
          was the
          <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">
           nominative case
          </a>
          of the word; the
          <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">
           accusative
          </a>
          form was originally
          <i>
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C3%BEe%C4%8B" title="wikt:þeċ">
            þeċ
           </a>
          </i>
          but over time the
          <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">
           dative
          </a>
          <i>
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C3%BEe" title="wikt:þe">
            þē
           </a>
          </i>
          replaced it.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-32">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-32">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <i>
           Þou
          </i>
          's accusative was at first spelled
          <i>
           þe
          </i>
          or
          <i>
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/the" title="wikt:the">
            the
           </a>
          </i>
          but later became
          <i>
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/thee" title="wikt:thee">
            thee
           </a>
          </i>
          .
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-33">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-33">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          There is a minor amount of T–V distinction among dialects of English that
          <i>
           (a)
          </i>
          employ informal first-person singulars (such as
          <a href="/wiki/South_Yorkshire" title="South Yorkshire">
           South Yorkshire
          </a>
          's continuing use of
          <i>
           tha
          </i>
          ) or
          <i>
           (b)
          </i>
          have adopted a new second-person plural (such as the
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_South" title="American South">
           American South
          </a>
          's
          <i>
           y'all
          </i>
          ). The non-
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prestige_dialect" title="Prestige dialect">
           prestige
          </a>
          nature of these dialects means that they
          <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">
           maintain a separate language register
          </a>
          (including
          <i>
           you
          </i>
          ) to be used among people outside their community whose judgment they are afraid of offending. For other variants, see the articles collected at
          <a href="/wiki/Category:Second-person_plural_pronouns_in_English" title="Category:Second-person plural pronouns in English">
           Category:Second-person plural pronouns in English
          </a>
          .
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-34">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-34">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>

         <span class="reference-text">

        <li id="cite_note-41">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-41">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Some members of the royal family insist upon it. During a 2010 interview with a
          <a href="/wiki/TV_2_(Denmark)" title="TV 2 (Denmark)">
           TV 2
          </a>
          journalist on board the training ship
          <a href="/wiki/Danmark_(ship)" title="Danmark (ship)">
           <i>
            Danmark
           </i>
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Prince_Joachim_of_Denmark" title="Prince Joachim of Denmark">
           Prince Joachim
          </a>
          pointedly refused to answer a question posed in the
          <i>
           du
          </i>
          form until the reporter rephrased it as
          <i>
           De
          </i>
          . The public debate then centered around whether the prince had demonstrated snobbishness, the journalist ignorance, or both.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
           <a href="#cite_note-39">
            [39]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
           <a href="#cite_note-40">
            [40]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-bork-42">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          ^
          <a href="#cite_ref-bork_42-0">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              a
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
          <a href="#cite_ref-bork_42-1">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              b
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Etik.dk.
          <i>
           <a class="external text" href="http://m.etik.dk/artikel/469393:Danmark--Hver-fjerde-dansker-vil-afskaffe--De" rel="nofollow">
            Hver fjerde dansker vil afskaffe 'De'
           </a>
          </i>
          ["One in four Danes want to abolish '
          <i>
           De
          </i>
          '"]. 6 July 2012.
          <span class="languageicon">
           (in Danish)
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-43">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-43">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          A 2012 survey found that only 6% of Danes would use
          <i>
           De
          </i>
          towards anyone they met and 16% would self-consciously never use it. However, 64% accepted its use towards members of the
          <a href="/wiki/Danish_royal_family" title="Danish royal family">
           Danish royal family
          </a>
          .
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bork_42-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-bork-42">
            [42]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-44">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-44">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          During debates at the
          <a href="/wiki/Folketing" title="Folketing">
           Folketing
          </a>
          , members are required to address one another in the third person by title or with the prefix of
          <i>
           hr.
          </i>
          (Mr.) or
          <i>
           fru
          </i>
          (Mrs.),
          <i>
           frøken
          </i>
          (Miss) having recently been given up. In debates away from the rostrum, however, they invariably default to
          <i>
           du
          </i>
          .
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-45">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-45">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          The same 2012 survey said 46% of Danes use
          <i>
           De
          </i>
          when speaking towards the elderly, out of respect. At the same time, the elderly were much more supportive of abolishing the word entirely.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bork_42-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-bork-42">
            [42]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>

         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

           </a>
          </i>
          , #1. 1998.
          <span class="languageicon">
           (in Danish)
          </span>

        <li id="cite_note-48">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-48">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          As especially polite alternatives, one may capitalize the pronoun to
          <i>
           Lei
          </i>
          or use
          <i>
           Ella
          </i>
          (lit. "She"); both sound quite archaic. If the pronoun is capitalized, the majuscule is applied to all its forms including the
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enclitic" title="Enclitic">
           enclitics
          </a>
          :
          <i>
           ...vorrei incontrarLa per parlarGliene
          </i>
          ("I would like to talk to you about it").
         </span>
        </li>

         <span class="reference-text">
          <span dir="rtl" lang="he">
           אורי אורבך, סבא שלי היה רב, 2002
          </span>
          ‎
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-63">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-63">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Zdic.net.
          <span lang="zh">
           《漢典》
          </span>
          [
          <i>
           Chinese Dictionary
          </i>
          ]. "
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.zdic.net/z/1a/js/6211.htm" rel="nofollow">
           <span lang="zh">
            我
           </span>
          </a>
          ". Accessed 21 August 2013.
          <span class="languageicon">
           (in Chinese)
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-64">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-64">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Although modern Chinese now distinguishes between
          <span lang="zh">
           你们
          </span>
          ("you" [plural]) and
          <span lang="zh">
           您
          </span>
          ("you" [cherished, respectful]), the legacy of
          <span lang="zh">
           您
          </span>
          's origin is still retained in the rarity of observing the form
          <span lang="zh">
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%82%A8%E4%BB%AC" title="wikt:您们">
            您们
           </a>
          </span>
          in Mandarin Chinese. Native speakers employ indirect phrasing like "everybody"　(
          <span lang="zh">
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A4%A7%E5%AE%B6" title="wikt:大家">
            大家
           </a>
          </span>
          ,
          <i>
           dàjiā
          </i>
          , lit. "big family") or "ladies and gentlemen" (
          <span lang="zh">
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%90%84%E4%BD%8D" title="wikt:各位">
            各位
           </a>
          </span>
          ,
          <i>
           gèwèi
          </i>
          , lit. "every seat") and
          <span lang="zh">
           您们
          </span>
          only infrequently appears in
          <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Mandarin" title="Taiwanese Mandarin">
           Taiwanese Mandarin
          </a>
          and among foreign students of the language.
          <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
           [
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
             <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">
              citation needed
             </span>
            </a>
           </i>
           ]
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-65">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-65">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation web">
           <a class="external text" href="http://bertilow.com/pmeg/gramatiko/pronomoj/dua.html" rel="nofollow">

            . Retrieved

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Dua+persono&amp;rft.pub=Bertilo&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fbertilow.com%2Fpmeg%2Fgramatiko%2Fpronomoj%2Fdua.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AT%E2%80%93V+distinction">
